قوله: «فتأمل»: كانه اشارة الى انه كان ينبغي ان يذكر نقيضيها كما ذكر عينيهما. فانه لو لم يرفع شيء منهما كان المركب ثابتاً والحال ان نقيض كل شي رفعه. انما قيد بذلك، لانه لا يجوز ان يكون نقيض المركب احد نقيضي الجزئين على التعيين، لجواز كذب المركب بالجزء الآخر فحينئذ يلزم اجتماع النقيضين على الكذب و ذلك باطل، مثلاً قولنا كل انسان حيوان بالفعل لا دائماً لو كان نقيضه نقيض الجزء الأول بعينه لزم اجتماعهما على الكذب ضرورة ان المركبة كاذبة بالجزء الاخير فانه اشارة الى قولنا: لاشيء من الانسان بحيوان بالفعل و هو كاذب قطعاً مع ان نقيض الجزء الاول وهو قولنا : ليس بعض الانسان بحيوان بالدوام كاذب ايضاً. تعليل للتقييد بمنع الخلو والحاصل: ان رفع احد الجزئين لا على التعيين وان كان معنى مشتركاً بين جميع اقسام الانفصال، الا انه لا يصح هنا الا الانفصال على سبيل منع الخلو وذلك لجواز ان يكون رفع المركب برفع كلا جزئيه فان المركب كما ينتفى بانتفاء احد اجزائه، كذلك ينتفي بانتفاء جميع اجزائه فحينئذ لا يجوز الانفصال الحقيقى والانفصال على سبيل منع الجمع لعدم جواز الجمع فيهما بخلافه على سبيل منع الخلو وايضاً يجوز في الانفصال على سبيل منع الجمع ان لا يرفع شيء منها كما هو ظاهر فحينئذ يكون المجموع ثابتاً هذا خلف. فان قيل : كما يجوز ان يكون رفع المركب برفع كلا جزئيه، فقد يكون برفع احدهما خاصة دون الأخر فحينئذ يكون الانفصال بينهما في الصدق والكذب معاً فلا يصح الرفع على سبيل منع الخلو. فالاولى ان يقال: على سبيل غير منع الجمع يصح في الكل فيقدر في بعض المواضع الانفصال الحقيقى وفي بعضها مانع الخلو. قلنا : قد سبق آنفاً ان مانع الخلو يستعمل على معنيين . احدهما اخص مقابل للانفصال الحقيقى وهو ما حكم فيه بالتنافي في الكذب لا في الصدق. وثانيهما اعم منه ومن الانفصال الحقيقى و هو ما حكم فيه بالتنافى فى الكذب مع قطع النظر عن الصدق اعم من ان يجتمعا في الصدق وان لا يجتمعا و هو المراد هنا فلا يلزم محذور
His statement: 'So reflect': It is as if it is an indication that he should have mentioned their two negations just as he mentioned their two affirmatives. For if nothing of them were removed, the compound would be established, while the negation of everything is its removal. It was restricted to that, because it is not permissible for the negation of the compound to be one of the two negations of the parts specifically, because of the possibility of the falsity of the compound by the other part; then the two contradictories would necessarily combine in falsity, and that is invalid. For example, our statement: 'Every human is an animal actually but not always' – if its negation were the negation of the first part specifically, they would necessarily combine in falsity, since the compound is false by the last part, for it refers to our statement: 'Nothing of humanity is an animal actually' which is absolutely false, while the negation of the first part, which is our statement: 'Not some human is an animal permanently', is also false. (This is) an explanation of the restriction to 'man' al-khuluw' (exclusive disjunction). The gist: The removal of one of the two parts unspecifically, although it is a common meaning among all forms of disjunction, yet here only the disjunction in the manner of 'man' al-khuluw' is valid. This is because it is possible that the removal of the compound is through the removal of both its parts; for the compound, just as it is negated by the negation of one of its parts, similarly it is negated by the negation of all its parts. So then the real disjunction and the disjunction in the manner of 'man' al-jam' (disjointness) are not allowed, because they do not allow combination (of both parts being false), unlike 'man' al-khuluw'. Also, in the disjunction in the manner of 'man' al-jam', it is permissible that nothing is removed from them, as is apparent, so then the total would be established – this is absurd. If it is said: Just as it is possible that the removal of the compound is by the removal of both its parts, it may also be by the removal of one of them exclusively without the other; then the disjunction between them would be in truth and falsity together, so the removal in the manner of 'man' al-khuluw' is not valid. So it is better to say: In the manner of non-'man' al-jam' it is valid in all cases, so in some places the real disjunction is estimated, and in others 'man' al-khuluw'. We say: It has already preceded that 'man' al-khuluw' is used in two senses. One is more specific, opposite to real disjunction, which is that in which incompatibility in falsity is judged, not in truth. The second is more general than it and than real disjunction, and it is that in which incompatibility in falsity is judged, regardless of truth, whether they combine in truth or not, and that is what is meant here, so no harm follows.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on a previous commentary regarding the negation of compound propositions. The author begins by pointing out a missing element: the commentator should have mentioned the two negations (of the parts) just as he mentioned the two affirmatives. The reason is given: if neither part is negated, the compound remains true, but the negation of a proposition is its removal. The commentary then explains why the negation of a compound cannot be simply the negation of one specific part. The risk is that the compound might be false due to the other part, leading to a situation where both the alleged negation and the negation of that specific part would be false together, which is impossible because two contradictories cannot both be false. An example is given: 'Every human is an animal actually but not always' (a compound of 'every human is an animal actually' and 'every human is an animal not always'). If we take its negation as the negation of the first part ('Nothing of humanity is an animal actually'), this is false, but the negation of the second part ('Not some human is an animal permanently') is also false, so both would be false simultaneously—impossible for a true negation. Therefore, the negation must be a disjunction that captures the possible ways the compound can be false. The discussion then focuses on why this disjunction must be of the type 'man' al-khuluw' (exclusive disjunction, where both cannot be false). The reason is that the compound can be false by either part being false or by both being false. Real disjunction (one true, one false) and 'man' al-jam' (disjointness, where both cannot be true but both can be false) are rejected: real disjunction because it requires opposite truth values, but here both can be false; 'man' al-jam' because it allows both to be false but also allows both to be true? Actually 'man' al-jam' is disjunction where both cannot be true, but both can be false. However, the author argues that if both parts are false, the compound is false, so the negation should also be true. But if both parts are false in 'man' al-jam', the negation is true? Wait, careful. The point is that the negation of the compound must cover the case where the compound is false. The compound is false if at least one part is false (since it's a conjunction? Actually the example is of a conditional? The compound is 'every human is an animal actually but not always', which is presumably a conjunction of 'every human is an animal actually' and 'every human is an animal not always'. But 'but not always' modifies the first? Actually it's a composite proposition. The negation of a conjunctive compound is a disjunction of the negations of the parts. So the negation of 'p and q' is 'not p or not q'. The author says the compound can be false by either part false or by both false. So the negation (the disjunction) should be true in all those cases. A real disjunction (exclusive or) would be false if both are true, which is fine, but also false if both are false? No, exclusive or is true only when exactly one is true. But here both parts could be false (both negations true), so exclusive or would be false in that case, which is invalid. Similarly, 'man' al-jam' (alternative opposition, where both cannot be true but both can be false) would allow both false (both negations true) but then the negation would be true? Actually in 'man' al-jam', the disjunction is true when both are false? In traditional logic, 'man' al-jam' is 'not both p and q', which is true if at least one is false. That is inclusive or. Wait, the terminology is confusing. In Islamic logic, 'man' al-khuluw' (exclusive disjunction) is 'either p or q but not both'. 'Man' al-jam' (disjointness) is 'either p is true or q is true but they cannot be both true' (i.e., exclusive disjunction but allowing both false? Actually standard: 'man' al-khuluw' = exclusive disjunction (one true, one false; not both true, not both false). 'Man' al-jam' = disjunction that prevents both being true but allows both being false (i.e., 'at most one true'). And 'man' al-khuluw wal-jam' = both exclusive and exhaustive? The author distinguishes. The argument: the negation of the compound must be a disjunction that is true when the compound is false. The compound is false in three cases: part1 false, part2 true; part1 true, part2 false; both false. So the disjunction (not p or not q) must be true in all these. In 'man' al-khuluw' (exclusive disjunction), the disjunction is false when both disjuncts are false (i.e., both parts true? No, disjuncts are negations; both negations false means both parts true, then compound true). But when both parts are false, both negations are true, so the exclusive disjunction (one true, one false) would be false? Actually exclusive disjunction of 'not p' and 'not q' would be true if exactly one of not-p or not-q is true. If both p and q are false, then both not-p and not-q are true, so exclusive disjunction is false. So exclusive disjunction fails when both parts are false. Hence the author says only 'man' al-khuluw' works, but explains that 'man' al-khuluw' can be interpreted in a broader sense that simply prevents both from being false (i.e., both cannot be false at the same time). That is the sense he intends. The passage then handles an objection that allows both parts to be false, and the reply distinguishes two meanings of 'man' al-khuluw': a specific one that opposes real disjunction (where they cannot both be false) and a general one that is simply an incompatibility in falsity regardless of truth—the latter is meant here. This resolves the issue. The commentary thus refines the earlier understanding by clarifying the precise type of disjunction required.

According to the passage, why can't the negation of a compound be the negation of a specific part?
A) Because it would make the compound true
B) Because it would lead to both the negation and the part's own negation being false simultaneously, which is impossible
C) Because the compound has only one part
D) Because the author says so without reason

Key Takeaway
The negation of a conjunctive compound must be an inclusive disjunction (man' al-khuluw in its broader sense) of the negations of its parts, allowing both to be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the correct negation of the compound proposition: 'Zakat is obligatory and fasting Ramadan is obligatory'. Explain why the negation cannot be simply 'Zakat is not obligatory' and why the disjunction must be of the man' al-khuluw type.
?
Identify the three ways the compound 'The prayer is valid only if ablution is performed' (interpreted as a conjunction: 'ablution performed and prayer valid'? Actually better: 'Prayer is valid and ablution is performed') can be false, and verify that the disjunction of negations covers all cases.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
5 min
The Two Senses of Man' al-Khuluw
Author resolves an objection by distinguishing two senses of man' al-khuluw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the specific and general meanings of man' al-khuluw and apply the correct one in the context of compound negation.

Core Idea
Man' al-khuluw has a specific sense (inclusive disjunction) and a general sense (simply 'not both false') – the latter is meant for compound negation.

After establishing that the negation of a compound must be a disjunction under man' al-khuluw, the author anticipates an objection: what if the compound is false by only one part? Then the disjunction would be a real disjunction (one true, one false), not man' al-khuluw. The author resolves this by clarifying that 'man' al-khuluw' is used in two senses: a specific one that contrasts with real disjunction, and a general one that simply means the two cannot both be false, regardless of truth. The general sense is intended, so the objection falls.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: The compound could be false by only one part, not both. In that case, the negation would be a disjunction where one disjunct is true and the other false – exactly like a real disjunction. So man' al-khuluw (which allows both true) would not apply.
The objection highlights a potential counterexample where the disjunction is not both false.
2
Recognize the need for a broader category
The negation must cover all falsity cases. If the disjunction is real (exclusive), it fails when both parts are false. So we need a type that covers both exclusive and inclusive cases. The author says man' al-khuluw in a broader sense fills this role.
The broader sense is simply the negation of both false – it does not impose an exclusivity condition.
3
Distinguish the two meanings
The specific sense (الاخص) of man' al-khuluw is opposite to real disjunction: it requires at least one true (i.e., both cannot be false) but does not prevent both from being true. The general sense (الاعم) is simply 'incompatibility in falsity' – they cannot both be false, without any additional condition on truth.
The general sense is equivalent to 'not both false', which is exactly what the negation requires.
4
Apply the general sense to resolve the objection
The general sense covers cases where the disjunction is exclusive (one true, one false) as well as both true – because in both cases, they are not both false. So even if the compound is false by only one part, the general man' al-khuluw holds, and the negation is valid.
Thus, the objection is answered: the intended meaning is the general sense.

The general sense of man' al-khuluw – simply that both cannot be false – is the correct one for negating compound propositions, as it covers all falsity scenarios.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the compound proposition 'The prayer is valid only if purity is present' (interpreted as a conditional, but for practice treat it as a conjunction: 'Purity is present and prayer is valid'). An opponent argues that the negation must be a real disjunction because the compound could be false by only one part. Use the distinction between the two senses of man' al-khuluw to refute this and affirm the correct negation.
?
Identify a compound statement from fiqh (e.g., 'The sale is valid and the price is paid') and determine whether its negation falls under the specific or general sense of man' al-khuluw, explaining why.

Metaphorical Negation: Disjunction as Contradictory
Author addresses an objection that a disjunction cannot be a real negation because it is affirmative, explaining it is a metaphorical negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a disjunctive proposition can function as the negation of a compound proposition despite being affirmative, and distinguish explicit negation from metaphorical negation using the criterion of logical equivalence.

Core Idea
A disjunction can be a negation when it is logically equivalent to the explicit contradictory.

You might think that only a negative proposition can serve as the contradictory of an affirmative. But in Islamic logic, a disjunction can sometimes act as a negation even though it is affirmative. This lesson clarifies how that works by appealing to logical equivalence rather than literal form.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule of contradiction
Start from the standard rule: for two propositions to be genuine contradictories (naqid sarih), they must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and quantity. This rule is foundational for determining valid contradictions.
If you need to review the conditions, refer to the lesson on tanāquḍ.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that a disjunctive proposition (man' al-khuluw) is always affirmative, and the compound proposition in the example is also affirmative. Since both are affirmative, they lack the required difference in quality, so the disjunction cannot be a contradictory of the compound.
The objector assumes only explicit contradiction (naqid sarih) counts as negation.
3
Understand the author's response
The author concedes that the disjunction is not an explicit contradictory. However, it is equivalent (musāwin) in truth conditions to the explicit negation. The explicit negation of the compound would be a single proposition like 'It is not the case that...' but the disjunction achieves the same truth-functional effect by presenting two alternatives that are jointly exhaustive and mutually exclusive in a specific way.
Equivalent means they always have the same truth value.
4
Learn the concept of metaphorical usage (tajawwuz)
Because the disjunction shares the truth conditions of the explicit negation, it is permissible to call it a 'negation' metaphorically. This is similar to how a general absolute proposition is sometimes called 'absolute' by extension. The terminology is not literal but based on a relevant relationship.
Metaphorical labeling is common in Islamic logic to extend terms to closely related cases.
5
Apply to a fresh example
Consider a compound proposition from usul al-fiqh: 'The ruling is that the contract is valid and obligatory.' Its explicit negation would be 'It is not the case that the contract is valid and obligatory.' This is equivalent to the disjunction: 'Either the contract is not valid, or if it is valid, it is not obligatory' (man' al-khuluw form).
Try to construct such disjunctions on your own for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «قضية منفصلة مانعة الخلو» خبر المبتداء اعنى قوله: «فنقيض قولنا ...». لا يقال ان المنفصلة المانعة الخلو تكون موجبة قطعا كما علم مما سبق فاذا كانت القضية المركبة ايضاً موجبة كما في هذا المثال فلا يصح ان يكون نقيضاً لها فان الاختلاف في الكيف شرط في التناقض كما تقدم. لانا نقول: هذا فى النقيض الصريح والمنفصلة ليست نقيضاً صريحاً للمركبة بل مساوية لنقيضها الصريح اعني: رفع احد الجزئين لا على التعيين على سبيل منع الخلو واطلاق اسم النقيض عليها على سبيل التجوز كإطلاقه على المطلقة العامة على ما سبق.
His statement: 'A disjunctive proposition of the type man' al-khuluw' is the predicate of the subject, i.e., his statement: 'So the negation of our statement...'. It cannot be said that the disjunctive of man' al-khuluw is affirmative, as known from what preceded, so if the compound proposition is also affirmative, as in this example, then it cannot be a negation of it, because difference in quality is a condition for contradiction, as previously. We say: This is regarding the explicit negation, and the disjunctive is not an explicit negation of the compound, but rather is equivalent to its explicit negation, i.e., the removal of one of the two parts unspecifically in the manner of man' al-khuluw. And the application of the name 'negation' to it is metaphorical, like its application to the general absolute as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage defends the claim that a disjunctive proposition of the type 'man' al-khuluw' can serve as the negation of a compound proposition. The objection is straightforward: for a proposition to be a contradictory, it must differ in quality (affirmative vs. negative). However, both the compound proposition (e.g., 'Every human is an animal actually but not always') and the proposed disjunction (e.g., 'Either not every human is an animal actually, or every human is an animal always'?) are affirmative. So how can the disjunction be a negation? The commentator's response is that the disjunction is not the literal (explicit) negation of the compound; rather, it is equivalent to the literal negation. The literal negation would be a single proposition like 'It is not the case that...' but that is not the form given. The disjunction is called 'negation' in a metaphorical sense, similar to how a general absolute proposition is sometimes called 'absolute' in a broad sense. The point is that the truth conditions match, so it is acceptable terminology.

Key Terms
النقيض الصريح
مساو
التجوز

C) It is the same proposition
D) It is negative in form

Key Takeaway
A disjunction can be a metaphorical negation when it is logically equivalent to the explicit contradictory, even though it differs in quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Negating Compound Modal Propositions
Author gives examples of negating compound modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to find the negation of any compound modal proposition by decomposing it into its simple parts and forming a disjunction of their negations.

Core Idea
The negation of a compound modal is a disjunction of the negations of its parts.

This passage applies a powerful rule: the negation of a compound proposition is equivalent to a disjunction (Arabic 'أو') of the negations of its simple components. The author demonstrates this with three classic modal compounds from Islamic logic: the non-permanent existential, the special possible, and the temporal proposition. Understanding this rule allows you to correctly invert complex modal statements used in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition
Locate the compound modal proposition whose negation you seek. In the passage, these are: al-wujudiyya al-la da'ima (non-permanent existential), al-mumkina al-khassa (special possible), and al-waqtiyya (temporal). Each is said to be 'composed' of two simpler propositions.
The composition here is conjunctive: the compound asserts both simple propositions simultaneously.
2
Decompose into simple parts
Determine the two simple propositions that constitute the compound. For the non-permanent existential, the parts are two general absolutes (mutlaqa 'amma). For the special possible, they are two general possibles (mumkina 'amma). For the temporal, one part is a temporal absolute (waqtiyya mutlaqa) and the other is a general absolute.
The general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma) is a proposition with no temporal or modal qualification, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'.
3
Find the negation of each simple part
Apply the standard negation rules known from earlier lessons: the negation of a general absolute is a permanent absolute (da'ima mutlaqa); the negation of a general possible is an absolute necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa); the negation of a temporal absolute is a temporal possible (mumkina waqtiyya).
These negations are themselves simple modal propositions.
4
Form the disjunction of the negations
The negation of the original compound is a disjunction (connective 'أو') of the two negations found in the previous step. For the non-permanent existential, the negation is 'either this permanent absolute or that permanent absolute' (i.e., the negation of the first part or the negation of the second part). Similarly for the others.
The disjunction is inclusive or? The passage does not specify, but in standard logic it is inclusive (at least one holds).
5
Verify with the given examples
Check that the author's statements match your application: for al-wujudiyya al-la da'ima, the negation is indeed a disjunction of two permanents; for al-mumkina al-khassa, a disjunction of two necessarys; for al-waqtiyya, a disjunction of a temporal possible and a permanent absolute.
The author says 'وهكذا البواقي' (and so on for the rest), implying the pattern generalises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «و انت بعد اطلاعك ...» مثلاً اذا علمت ان الوجودية اللادائمة مركبة من مطلقتين عامتين و ان نقيض المطلقة العامة الدائمة المطلقة، علمت ان نقيض الوجودية اللادائمة اما هذه الدائمة او تلك الدائمة و اذا علمت ان الممكنة الخاصة مركبة من ممكنتين عامتين وان نقيض الممكنة العامة الضرورية المطلقة علمت ان نقيض الممكنة الخاصة اما هذه الضرورية أو تلك الضرورية و اذا علمت ان الوقتية مركبة من وقتية مطلقة و مطلقة عامة و ان نقيض الوقتية المطلقة الممكنة الوقتية و نقيض المطلقة العامة الدائمة المطلقة، علمت ان نقيض الوقتية اما الممكنة الوقتية او الدائمة المطلقة وهكذا البواقي
His statement: 'And you, after your knowledge...' For example, if you know that the non-permanent existential proposition is composed of two general absolute propositions, and that the negation of the general absolute is the permanent absolute, then you know that the negation of the non-permanent existential is either this permanent or that permanent. And if you know that the special possible proposition is composed of two general possible propositions, and that the negation of the general possible is the absolute necessary, then you know that the negation of the special possible is either this necessary or that necessary. And if you know that the temporal proposition is composed of a temporal absolute and a general absolute, and that the negation of the temporal absolute is the temporal possible, and the negation of the general absolute is the permanent absolute, then you know that the negation of the temporal is either the temporal possible or the permanent absolute. And so on for the rest.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of the rule discussed earlier: the negation of a compound proposition is a disjunction of the negations of its parts. The examples involve modal propositions common in Islamic logic. First, the 'non-permanent existential' (al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima) is a compound of two general absolute propositions (e.g., 'every human is an animal actually' and 'every human is an animal not always'? Actually the composition is not fully explained here, but the reader is assumed to know that the negation of a general absolute is a permanent absolute (dā'ima muṭlaqa). So the negation of the non-permanent existential is 'either this permanent or that permanent' (i.e., either the negation of the first part or the negation of the second part). Similarly, the 'special possible' (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa) is composed of two general possible propositions, and the negation of a general possible is the absolute necessary. So its negation is a disjunction of two necessary propositions. The 'temporal' (al-waqtiyya) is composed of a temporal absolute and a general absolute; its negation is a disjunction of the temporal possible and the permanent absolute. The pattern is consistent: find the negations of the simple parts, then form a disjunction of those, which will be the equivalent of the literal negation of the compound.

Key Terms
الوجودية اللادائمة
المطلقة العامة
الدائمة المطلقة
الممكنة الخاصة
الوقتية

Key Takeaway
To negate a compound modal proposition, decompose it into its simple parts, find the negation of each part, and combine them with a disjunction ('or').

Why a Particular Compound Proposition is Necessarily False
Author explains why a particular compound proposition is false and addresses an objection about subject determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a particular compound proposition with a non-permanent condition is false, and defend this analysis against the objection that the subject is indeterminate.

Core Idea
A compound proposition's subject must be determinate or contradiction arises.

In this passage, the author shows that the proposition 'Some animal is actually human' becomes false when modified by a non-permanent condition. The key is that the non-permanent condition introduces a contradictory negative proposition, and the subject must refer to the same individuals. Otherwise, the two parts could both be true separately. This lesson walks you through the logical structure and the refutation of a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition and its parts
Locate the example: 'Some animal is actually human' with a non-permanent condition. Recognize that the compound consists of an affirmative part and a implied negative part from the modal condition.
2
Understand the effect of the non-permanent condition
The author says the non-permanent condition indicates a general absolute that differs in quality (so negative) but agrees in quantity (particular). Thus it implies 'Not some animal is actually human'.
This is an immediate inference from the modal compound.
3
Recognize the requirement for a single determinate subject in compounds
In a compound proposition, the subject must be taken as referring to the same specific individuals; otherwise, the parts could be true of different individuals. The objection incorrectly assumes the subject is conceptual only.
This is unlike separate propositions where only agreement in wording suffices.
4
Apply the argument to show the compound is false
Given the subject is determinate, the affirmative and negative parts contradict each other. Hence the compound cannot be true. The author also shows that both possible interpretations of the subject lead to absurdities.
This proves the falsity by reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «قد تكذب المركبة الجزئية كقولنا بعض الحيوان انسان بالفعل...»: انما كذبت، لان اللادوام اشارة الى مطلقة عامة مخالفة للاصل في الكيف موافقة في الكم، ففى هذا المثالي يكون إشارة الى قولنا : ليس بعض الحيوان بانسان بالفعل فيكون واحد منهما كاذباً قطعاً والا لزم اثبات الشيء وسلبه بالنسبة الى شيء واحد فان المراد من بعض الحيوان الذى جعل موضوعاً اما ان يكون من افراد الحيوان الناطق اوغيره فعلى الاول يكذب الجزء الثانى و على الثاني يكذب الجزء الاول. فان قيل : قد سبق في اوائل المبحث ان تعيين الموضوع امر خارج عن المفهوم و النظر في جميع الاحكام انما هو الى مفهوم الجزئيتين اعنى الايجاب لبعض الافراد و السلب عن البعض فحينئذ لا يكذب شيء منهما كما هو ظاهر. قلنا : هذا لا يجرى فى المركبات لان الموضوع فيها يجب ان يكون امراً واحداً معيناً كما سبق فانها في حكم قضية واحدة، بخلاف القضايا المتعددة، فانه يكفي فيها في اتحاد الموضوع اتحاده في اللفظ فتأمل فانه بحث نفيس. اما الاول فلانه يستلزم سلب الاخص عن الاعم و اما الثانى فلانه يستلزم صدق الاخص على جميع أفراد الاعم و كلاهما باطل.
His statement: 'The particular compound may be false, like our statement: Some animal is actually human...' It is false only because the non-permanent indicates a general absolute that differs from the original in quality but agrees in quantity. So in this example, it indicates our statement: 'Not some animal is actually human', so one of them is absolutely false, otherwise it would necessitate affirming and negating something with respect to one thing. For the intended meaning of 'some animal' made the subject is either from the individuals of rational animal or not. On the first, the second part is false; on the second, the first part is false. If it is said: It has already preceded in the beginning of the topic that the determination of the subject is outside the concept, and the consideration in all judgments is only towards the concept of the two particulars, i.e., the affirmation of some individuals and the negation of some, so then none of them is false, as is apparent. We say: This does not apply to compounds, because in them the subject must be one determinate thing, as preceded, for they are in the ruling of a single proposition, unlike multiple propositions, where sufficiency in the unity of subject is its unity in wording. So reflect, for it is a subtle investigation. As for the first, because it necessitates the negation of the more specific from the more general; and as for the second, because it necessitates the truth of the more specific over all individuals of the more general; and both are invalid.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage discusses the falsity of a particular compound proposition: 'Some animal is actually human.' The compound is formed by adding a temporal condition (non-permanent) to a particular affirmative. The author explains that it is false because the non-permanent part effectively introduces a corresponding negative proposition ('Not some animal is actually human') that contradicts the first part. The crucial point is that in a compound proposition, the subject must be interpreted as a single determinate referent; otherwise, the two parts could refer to different individuals and both be true. The objection claims that in quantified propositions, the subject is not determinate but refers to 'some individuals' conceptually, so both parts could be true of different individuals. The reply distinguishes compound propositions (which function as a single proposition) from separate propositions; in a compound, the subject must be the same actual individuals. Therefore, the compound is false. The final lines (30) further justify why the two possible interpretations both lead to absurdity: either the subject includes all rational animals (leading to negating the more specific from the more general, i.e., human from animal, which is false) or includes non-rational animals (leading to affirming human of all animals, also false). This reinforces that the compound cannot be true.

Key Terms
المركبة الجزئية (al-murakkaba al-juz'iyya): A compound proposition with particular quantification, e.g., 'Some animal is actually human but not always' (though not fully stated).
اللادوام (al-la dawam): The non-permanent modal condition; it changes the proposition from a simple to a compound by adding a temporal negation.
تعيين الموضوع (ta'yin al-mawdu'): Determination of the subject to specific individuals; in compounds, the subject must be identified, unlike in separate propositions where only the concept matters.

Key Takeaway
A particular compound proposition with a non-permanent modal is necessarily false because the modal implies its contradictory, and the subject must be taken as determinate.

Negating a Particular Compound Proposition
Author explains the method for negating a particular compound proposition by universalizing the subject and using a disjunction of partial negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the negation of a particular compound proposition by taking the subject universally and forming a disjunction of the two partial negations.

Core Idea
Negate the compound by universalizing the subject and disjoining the two partial negations.

In classical Islamic logic, negating a proposition that has two parts under a particular quantifier requires a precise method. Simply negating each part individually fails to capture the full contradictory. The author of our text shows that the subject must be taken universally, and the negation is expressed as a disjunction between the two partial negations. This ensures that every individual is accounted for. For example, the negation of 'Some animal is human permanently or not permanently' leads to a universal statement disjoining the two possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular compound proposition
Find a proposition with a particular quantifier (e.g., 'some') that has two parts connected by 'and' or 'or' with temporal modes. For instance, 'Some animal is not human permanently or some animal is human permanently' is a compound.
The compound may involve two predicates or two modes.
2
Take the subject universally
Instead of using the original particular quantifier, consider the subject in a universal sense: 'every individual of the subject'. This is necessary to cover all individuals.
The author explicitly states: 'يؤخذ الموضوع كلياً' – the subject must be taken universally.
3
Attribute the predicate to each individual with the two modes
For each individual, apply the two partial negations (the negations of each part of the compound) with their respective temporal modes (e.g., permanently, not permanently). This yields a disjunction between the two partial negations.
The two partial negations are the contradictory of each part of the original compound.
4
Formulate the resulting universal disjunctive proposition
The result is a universal affirmative categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate, e.g., 'Every animal is either not human permanently or some are human permanently and others not.' This is the negation of the original particular compound.
The author notes that this disjunction is equivalent to the technical negation, not identical.
5
Verify the three possible concepts
The author identifies three possibilities for the individuals: (1) all are not human permanently, (2) some are human permanently and others not, or (3) a combination. The disjunction captures all mutually exclusive cases that together contradict the original compound.
This three-part disjunction is a clear demonstration of the method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «ان توضع افراد الموضوع كلها ...» اى لابد فى طريق اخذ النقيض للمركبة الجزئية ان يؤخذ الموضوع كلياً ثم ينسب محمول تلك المركبة الى كل واحد واحد من افراده ايجاباً وسلبا موجهاً بجهتى نقيض جزئى المركبة و هذا هو المراد بالترديد بين نقيضي الجزئين والا فينتقض الجزئين قضيتان و لم يقع الترديد بينهما اصلاً. قوله : «و يقال في المثال المذكور كل حيوان ... اعلم ان هذا يشتمل على مفهومات ثلاث لان كل واحد واحد من افراد الحيوان اما ان يكون انساناً دائماً و اما ان لا يكون انساناً دائماً اما ان لا يكون واحد منها انساناً دائماً او كان بعضه انساناً دائماً دون بعض. و بعبارة اوضح اما ان يكون الانسانية مسلوباً عن كل واحد واحد او مسلوباً عن البعض دائماً ثابتاً للبعض دائماً فالجزء الثانى مشتمل على مفهومين. فقد ظهر من ذلك انه يمكن اخذ نقيض المركبة الجزئية بطريق آخر وهو ان تركب منفصلة مانعة الخلو من هذه المفهومات الثلاث لكن لا يكون نقيضاً اصطلاحياً بل مساوياً للنقيض الاصطلاحي فافهم. و قد عرفت آنفاً ان المنفصلة ليست نقيضاً صريحاً للمركبة بل مساوية لنقيضها الصريح فلا بأس هيهنا ايضاً بذلك و كان الامر بالفهم لذلك فليتنبه. قوله وهى قضية حملية مرددة المحمول: اى حملية موجبة كلية، و بما سبق آنفاً لا يرد ان
His statement: 'That the individuals of the subject are all placed...' i.e., it is necessary in the method of taking the negation of the particular compound that the subject be taken universally, then the predicate of that compound is attributed to every single one of its individuals affirmatively and negatively, qualified by the two modes of the two partial negations of the compound. And this is what is meant by the disjunction between the two negations of the two parts; otherwise, the two particulars become two propositions, and the disjunction does not occur between them at all. His statement: 'And it is said in the mentioned example: Every animal...' Know that this includes three concepts, because every single one of the individuals of animal is either human permanently, or not human permanently, or either not one of them is human permanently, or some of it are human permanently and others not. In a clearer expression: either humanity is negated from every single one, or it is negated from some permanently and affirmed for others permanently. So the second part contains two concepts. It appears from this that it is possible to take the negation of the particular compound by another method: that a disjunctive proposition of man' al-khuluw is composed from these three concepts, but it is not a technical negation, rather it is equivalent to the technical negation. So understand. And you have already known that the disjunctive is not an explicit negation of the compound but is equivalent to its explicit negation, so there is no harm here as well. And the command to understand is for that, so let one be cautious. His statement: 'And it is a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate' i.e., a universal affirmative categorical, and by what preceded, it does not refute that...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of negating particular compound propositions. The author states that when forming the negation, one must take the subject universally (i.e., consider all individuals) and then attribute the predicate to each individual with the two modes corresponding to the negations of the parts. This results in a disjunction between the two partial negations. Otherwise, if the subject is not taken universally, the two parts would be separate propositions without a genuine disjunction. Next, an example is given with the statement 'Every animal...' (probably an example like 'Some animal is human actually but not always'?). The author identifies three concepts (or three possibilities) for the individuals of animal: (1) every one is human permanently (this would be the affirmative part), (2) not one is human permanently (negative part), and (3) some are permanently human and others not (a mixed case). But actually the text says 'either every one is human permanently, or every one is not human permanently, or either not one is permanently human or some are permanently human and others not' – this is a bit confusing. He clarifies that there are three concepts: either humanity is negated from all, or it is negated from some permanently and affirmed for others permanently. This shows that the negation of the particular compound can be expressed as a disjunction of three mutually exclusive possibilities (a three-part exclusive disjunction). However, this disjunction is not the literal (technical) contradictory of the compound but is equivalent to it. The author reiterates the earlier point that such a disjunction is not a literal contradictory but an equivalent substitute. Finally, he identifies the resulting proposition as a universal affirmative categorical with a disjunctive predicate (e.g., 'Every animal is either not human permanently or...' etc.). The passage ends abruptly but the point is made.

Key Terms

الترديد بين نقيضي الجزئين (al-tardid bayn naqiday al-juz'ayn)
حملية مرددة المحمول (hamaliyya muraddada al-mahmul)

Negating a particular compound requires universalizing the subject and disjoining the two partial negations, yielding a universal affirmative proposition with a disjunctive predicate.

Why Disjunctives Have No Beneficial Conversion
Analyzes whether conversion applies to disjunctive propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why disjunctive propositions are not subject to beneficial conversion and respond to an objection using the concept of meaningful substitution.

Core Idea
Swapping terms in disjunctives does not change the meaning, so conversion is meaningless.

In this lesson, we delve into a subtle dialectical exchange from the hashya tradition. The commentator Mirza Muhammad Ali addresses an apparent ambiguity in the definition of conversion and refutes an objection that would allow disjunctive conversion. This sharpens our understanding of what makes a conversion 'beneficial' (mu'tabar).

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of conversion
Conversion ('aks mustawi) is traditionally defined as swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality. Some logicians extend it to all propositions, including conditionals.
2
Identify the ambiguity in 'the two terms'
If 'the two terms' means the actual essences (dhat) of subject and predicate, then even categorical conversion doesn't fit because the essence becomes predicate. If it means the terms as verbally mentioned, then disjunctives would have conversion since their terms are distinct in mention.
This is the key ambiguity that drives the dialectic.
3
Analyze the objection
An objector argues that swapping terms changes the judgment: 'Either the number is even or odd' judges evenness as incompatible with oddness, while the converse judges oddness as incompatible with evenness — different concepts.
This objection seems to show a change in meaning.
4
Evaluate the answer
Mirza Muhammad Ali concedes the conceptual difference but argues the net result reduces to the same mutual incompatibility. Thus there is no beneficial change in meaning.
The conversion is not 'meaningful' (tabdil ma'nawi).
5
Conclude the dialectic
Therefore, scholars ruled that disjunctives have no beneficial conversion. The term 'substitution' in conversion refers to swapping the descriptive titles ('unwan), not the essences.
This refinement preserves the definition of conversion while excluding disjunctives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله سواء كان الطرفان ...»: الغرض من هذا التعميم هو: ان العكس المستوى يجرى في : كل من الحمليات والشرطيات ولا يختص بالحمليات كما يظهر بعضهم حيث عرفه بتبديل كل من الموضوع والمحمول. ی بقى هنا شيء و هو انه ان اريد بالطرفين طرفا القضية في الحقيقة لم يدخل في التعريف شيء من عكس الحمليات لان الطرفين في الحقيقة في الحمليات هو ذات الموضوع و وصف المحمول و في العكس لا تصير ذات الموضوع محمولاً ووصف الموضوع موضوعاً بل يصير وصف الموضوع محمولاً و ذات المحمول موضوعاً كما هو ظاهر. و ان اريد طرفا القضية فى الذكر يلزم ان يكون للمنفصلات عكس، لان طرفيها وان لم يكونا متميزين بحسب الطبع لكنها متميزان فى الذكر و الحال ان القوم صرحوا بانها لا عكس لها . والجواب بعد تسليم الشق الثانى ان المراد بالتبديل التبديل المعنوى المغير للمعنى ولاشك ان هذا المعنى لا يحصل فى المنفصلات لظهور ان معنى المنفصلة لا يتغير بحسب التبديل اذ معناها هو المعاندة بين الشيئين سواء بدلا طرفاها ام لا . فان قلت لا نسلم عدم تغير المعنى فى المنفصلة بتبديل الطرفين لظهور ان المفهوم من قولنا: اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً و اما ان يكون فرداً هو الحكم على زوجية العدد بمعاندة الفردية و المفهوم من قولنا: اما ان يكون العدد فرداً و اما ان يكون زوجاً هو الحكم على فردية العدد بمعاندة الزوجية ولاشك في تغاير هذين المفهومين فان المفهوم من معاندة هذا لذاك غير المفهوم من معاندة ذاك لهذا. قلت نعم ولكن يرجع محصل المفهومين الى شيء واحد و هو المعاندة بين الشيئين فلافايدة يعتد بها في انعكاسها فلذا حكم القوم بان المنفصلة لاعكس لها اى العكس المعتد به فتأمل (میرزا محمد علی) ثم ليس المراد من تبديل الموضوع و المحمول تبديل الذات التي هي قال الشيخ عبد الرحيم ره): «... الموضوع فى الحقيقة و الوصف الذي هو المحمول بل المراد تبديل عنوانها.
[Commenting on] his saying 'whether the two terms ...': The purpose of this generalization is that ordinary conversion applies to both categoricals and conditionals, and is not specific to categoricals as some appear to think, where they defined it as the substitution of both subject and predicate. There remains here something: if by 'the two terms' is meant the actual terms of the proposition [in reality], then nothing of the conversion of categoricals enters into the definition, because the actual terms in categoricals are the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate, and in conversion the essence of the subject does not become the predicate nor the description of the subject become the subject; rather, the description of the subject becomes the predicate and the essence of the predicate becomes the subject, as is apparent. And if by 'two terms' is meant the terms in [verbal] mention, then it would follow that disjunctives have a conversion, because their two terms, although not distinct by nature, are distinct in mention — yet the scholars have stated that they have no conversion. The answer, after conceding the second branch, is that what is meant by 'substitution' is a meaningful substitution that changes the meaning, and there is no doubt that this meaning does not occur in disjunctives, because it is apparent that the meaning of a disjunctive does not change by substitution, since its meaning is the incompatibility between the two things, whether the two terms are swapped or not. If you say: We do not concede that there is no change of meaning in the disjunctive by swapping the two terms, because it is apparent that the concept of our saying 'Either the number is even or odd' is the judgment of the number's evenness as incompatible with oddness, and the concept of our saying 'Either the number is odd or even' is the judgment of the number's oddness as incompatible with evenness, and there is no doubt that these two concepts are different, for the concept of 'incompatibility of this with that' is other than the concept of 'incompatibility of that with this.' I say: Yes, but the net result of the two concepts reverts to one thing, which is the incompatibility between the two things, so there is no benefit to be considered in its conversion. Therefore the scholars ruled that the disjunctive has no conversion, i.e., no beneficial conversion. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) Then what is meant by 'substitution of subject and predicate' is not the substitution of the essence that is the subject in reality and the description that is the predicate, but rather the substitution of their [descriptive] titles.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on whether ordinary conversion ('aks mustawi) applies to disjunctive propositions. The author, Mirza Muhammad Ali, is commenting on a line that generalizes conversion to both categoricals and conditionals. He first notes that some logicians define conversion as swapping subject and predicate. Then he presents a potential ambiguity: if by 'terms' we mean the actual essences (dhat) in reality, then even categorical conversion would not fit the definition because in conversion, the essence of the subject becomes predicate? Actually, in categoricals, the subject-term is the essence and the predicate-term is a description; after conversion, the description becomes subject and the essence becomes predicate, so the 'terms' in reality are not simply swapped. If instead we mean the terms as verbally mentioned, then disjunctives would have a conversion, because their two terms are distinct in mention, yet scholars say disjunctives have no conversion. The answer is that conversion must change the meaning, but in disjunctives, swapping terms does not change the core meaning of mutual incompatibility. An objector raises that there is a difference: 'Either the number is even or odd' judges evenness as incompatible with oddness, while 'Either the number is odd or even' judges oddness as incompatible with evenness. The reply concedes this but argues the net result is the same incompatibility, so the conversion is not beneficial. Thus the scholars rule that disjunctives have no beneficial conversion. The author concludes that 'substitution' refers to swapping the titles ('unwan) of subject and predicate, not the essences themselves.

The Dilemma of the Two Terms in Simple Conversion
Author identifies a dilemma in defining 'aks mustawi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two interpretations of 'the two terms' in the definition of simple conversion and explain the problems each interpretation creates.

Core Idea
The definition of simple conversion fails for categoricals if 'terms' are real, and for exclusive disjunctions if 'terms' are mentioned.

When defining 'aks mustawi as 'substitution of the two terms', we must ask: which two terms? The author presents a dilemma: the real terms (essence and description) or the mentioned terms (as written)? Each leads to a flaw, as categorical conversion doesn't fit the first, and exclusive disjunctions would be convertible under the second, contradicting scholarly consensus. This dilemma motivates a more precise definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition
The author notes that the definition generalizes to both categoricals and conditionals. The definition is 'substitution of the two terms' (تبديل الطرفين).
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
Interpretation 1: 'the two terms in reality' (طرفا القضية في الحقيقة) – meaning the real subject (essence) and real predicate (description). Interpretation 2: 'the two terms in mention' (طرفا القضية في الذكر) – meaning the words as they appear.
The real subject is the essence (ذات), the real predicate is the description (وصف المحمول).
3
Apply the real interpretation to categoricals
If 'terms' means real terms, then in conversion, the essence of the subject does not become the predicate, nor the description of the subject the subject. Instead, the description of the subject becomes the predicate and the essence of the predicate becomes the subject. Thus, categorical conversion would not fit the definition.
Example: 'Every human is rational' – real subject is 'human', real predicate is 'rationality'. After conversion: 'Some rational beings are humans' – the real subject becomes 'rational being' which is the description, not the essence.
4
Apply the mention interpretation to exclusive disjunctions
If 'terms' means mentioned terms, then exclusive disjunctions have distinct mentioned terms (e.g., 'even' and 'odd'), so they would satisfy the definition and have a converse. But scholars explicitly say exclusive disjunctions have no converse, creating a contradiction.
The author points out this problem to show the definition is inadequate.
5
Summarize the dilemma
Both interpretations lead to unacceptable consequences: the real interpretation excludes valid categorical conversions, and the mention interpretation includes invalid conversions of exclusive disjunctions. Therefore, the definition needs refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله سواء كان الطرفان ... الغرض من هذا التعميم هو: ان العكس المستوى يجرى في كل من الحمليات والشرطيات ولا يختص بالحمليات كما يظهر بعضهم حيث عرفه بتبديل كل من الموضوع والمحمول. يبقى هنا شيء و هو انه ان اريد بالطرفين طرفا القضية في الحقيقة لم يدخل في التعريف شيء من عكس الحمليات لان الطرفين في الحقيقة في الحمليات هو ذات الموضوع و وصف المحمول و في العكس لا تصير ذات الموضوع محمولاً ووصف الموضوع موضوعاً بل يصير وصف الموضوع محمولاً و ذات المحمول موضوعاً كما هو ظاهر. و ان اريد طرفا القضية فى الذكر يلزم ان يكون للمنفصلات عكس، لان طرفيها وان لم يكونا متميزين بحسب الطبع لكنها متميزان فى الذكر و الحال ان القوم صرحوا بانها لا عكس لها . والجواب بعد تسليم الشق الثانى ان المراد بالتبديل التبديل المعنوى المغير للمعنى ولاشك ان هذا المعنى لا يحصل فى المنفصلات لظهور ان معنى المنفصلة لا يتغير بحسب التبديل اذ معناها هو المعاندة بين الشيئين سواء بدلا طرفاها ام لا . فان قلت لا نسلم عدم تغير المعنى فى المنفصلة بتبديل الطرفين لظهور ان المفهوم من قولنا: اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً و اما ان يكون فرداً هو الحكم على زوجية العدد بمعاندة الفردية و المفهوم من قولنا: اما ان يكون العدد فرداً و اما ان يكون زوجاً هو الحكم على فردية العدد بمعاندة الزوجية ولاشك في تغاير هذين المفهومين فان المفهوم من معاندة هذا لذاك غير المفهوم من معاندة ذاك لهذا. قلت نعم ولكن يرجع محصل المفهومين الى شيء واحد و هو المعاندة بين الشيئين فلافايدة يعتد بها في انعكاسها فلذا حكم القوم بان المنفصلة لاعكس لها اى العكس المعتد به فتأمل. ثم ليس المراد من تبديل الموضوع و المحمول تبديل الذات التي هي الموضوع فى الحقيقة و الوصف الذي هو المحمول بل المراد تبديل عنوانها.
His statement: 'whether the two terms...' The purpose of this generalization is that simple conversion applies to both categoricals and conditionals and is not specific to categoricals, as some appear to think, who defined it as the substitution of both the subject and predicate. There remains something here: if by 'the two terms' is meant the two terms of the proposition in reality, then none of the conversion of categoricals enters the definition, because the two terms in reality in categoricals are the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate, and in conversion the essence of the subject does not become the predicate nor the description of the subject the subject; rather, the description of the subject becomes the predicate and the essence of the predicate becomes the subject, as is apparent. And if by 'the two terms' is meant the two terms in mention, then exclusive disjunctions would have a converse, because their two terms, even if not distinct by nature, are distinct in mention, yet the scholars have stated that they have no converse. The answer, after conceding the second horn, is that what is meant by substitution is the meaningful substitution that changes the meaning, and there is no doubt that this meaning is not obtained in exclusive disjunctions, because it is apparent that the meaning of the exclusive disjunction does not change by substitution, since its meaning is the mutual opposition between the two things, whether its two terms are switched or not. If you say: We do not concede the non-change of meaning in the exclusive disjunction by switching the two terms, because it is apparent that the meaning of our statement: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' is the judgment that the number's evenness is mutually opposed to oddness, and the meaning of our statement: 'Either the number is odd or it is even' is the judgment that the number's oddness is mutually opposed to evenness, and there is no doubt that these two meanings are different, for the meaning of the opposition of this to that is not the same as the meaning of the opposition of that to this. I say: Yes, but the upshot of the two meanings returns to one thing, namely the mutual opposition between the two things, so there is no benefit worth considering in their conversion; therefore the scholars judged that the exclusive disjunction has no converse — i.e., no converse worth considering. So reflect. Then what is meant by the substitution of the subject and predicate is not the substitution of the essence that is the subject in reality and the description that is the predicate; rather, it is the substitution of their title.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical expansion of the definition of 'aks mustawi (simple conversion). The author begins by noting that the definition (given by the original text) generalizes to both categorical and conditional propositions, contrary to some who restrict it to categoricals. He then identifies a problem: if 'the two terms' refers to the real terms (essence of subject, description of predicate), then categorical conversion would not be covered because in conversion the essence of the subject does not become the predicate nor the description of the subject the subject; rather, the description of the subject becomes the predicate and the essence of the predicate becomes the subject. If instead 'the two terms' refers to the terms as mentioned, then exclusive disjunctions would have a converse, since their terms are distinct in mention, but the scholars (al-qawm) explicitly say they have no converse. The author resolves this by conceding the second interpretation and clarifying that substitution must be meaningful, i.e., it changes the meaning of the proposition. In exclusive disjunctions, switching the terms does not change the meaning because the core is opposition between the two things; thus there is no benefit to such conversion. An objection is raised: switching the terms does change the meaning because the emphasis shifts from one term to the other. The response: the upshot of both meanings is the same mutual opposition, so no real benefit. Hence the scholars say exclusive disjunctions have no converse worth considering. Finally, the author refines the definition: substitution is not of the real essence and description but of their 'unwan (title/indication). This ensures the definition covers all valid conversions.

المنفصلة

Resolving the Dilemma: Meaningful Substitution and the Role of al-'Unwan
Author resolves the dilemma and refines the definition of 'aks mustawi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why exclusive disjunctions have no meaningful converse and how the refinement to 'tabdil al-'unwan' resolves the definitional dilemma of simple conversion.

Core Idea
Simple conversion requires a change in meaning; exclusive disjunctions fail this test, and substitution is of the title, not the real essence.

Having identified the dilemma, the author now resolves it by conceding the second interpretation (terms in mention) and clarifying that substitution must be meaningful—i.e., it must change the proposition's meaning. Exclusive disjunctions, despite having distinct mentioned terms, do not change meaning when swapped, so they lack a beneficial converse. An objection is raised that switching terms changes the emphasis, but the author shows the core meaning is identical. Finally, he refines: substitution is of the 'title' (al-'unwan), which covers categorical conversions and excludes exclusive disjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's answer
The author concedes the second horn (terms in mention) but adds a condition: substitution must be meaningful, i.e., it must change the meaning of the proposition. This is the key refinement.
The author uses 'التبديل المعنوى المغير للمعنى'.
2
Apply the condition to exclusive disjunctions
Check if swapping the terms in an exclusive disjunction changes the meaning. Example: 'Either the number is even or odd' vs. 'Either the number is odd or even'. The meaning is still mutual opposition between evenness and oddness. Therefore, no meaningful change occurs.
The condition is that the conversion must produce a new meaning.
3
Examine the objection
An objector argues that the meanings are different because the judgment is about different subjects: one judges that evenness opposes oddness, the other that oddness opposes evenness. This seems like different emphases.
The objector's point: the logical subject changes.
4
Follow the author's response
The author agrees there is a difference in wording but asserts that the upshot (محصل المفهومين) of both is the same—mutual opposition between the two things. Hence, there is no benefit worth considering in such conversion. Therefore, scholars say exclusive disjunctions have no 'converse worth considering'.
The author uses 'فلا فائدة يعتد بها'.
5
Understand the final refinement: tabdil al-'unwan
The author concludes that substitution is not of the real essence and description, but of their 'title' (عنوان). This means we swap the labels, not the logical subjects/predicates in reality. This covers categorical conversion (where description becomes predicate) and excludes exclusive disjunctions (since no meaning change).
'تبديل العنوان' is the precise term that resolves the dilemma.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله سواء كان الطرفان ... الغرض من هذا التعميم هو: ان العكس المستوى يجرى في كل من الحمليات والشرطيات ولا يختص بالحمليات كما يظهر بعضهم حيث عرفه بتبديل كل من الموضوع والمحمول. يبقى هنا شيء و هو انه ان اريد بالطرفين طرفا القضية في الحقيقة لم يدخل في التعريف شيء من عكس الحمليات لان الطرفين في الحقيقة في الحمليات هو ذات الموضوع و وصف المحمول و في العكس لا تصير ذات الموضوع محمولاً ووصف الموضوع موضوعاً بل يصير وصف الموضوع محمولاً و ذات المحمول موضوعاً كما هو ظاهر. و ان اريد طرفا القضية فى الذكر يلزم ان يكون للمنفصلات عكس، لان طرفيها وان لم يكونا متميزين بحسب الطبع لكنها متميزان فى الذكر و الحال ان القوم صرحوا بانها لا عكس لها . والجواب بعد تسليم الشق الثانى ان المراد بالتبديل التبديل المعنوى المغير للمعنى ولاشك ان هذا المعنى لا يحصل فى المنفصلات لظهور ان معنى المنفصلة لا يتغير بحسب التبديل اذ معناها هو المعاندة بين الشيئين سواء بدلا طرفاها ام لا . فان قلت لا نسلم عدم تغير المعنى فى المنفصلة بتبديل الطرفين لظهور ان المفهوم من قولنا: اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً و اما ان يكون فرداً هو الحكم على زوجية العدد بمعاندة الفردية و المفهوم من قولنا: اما ان يكون العدد فرداً و اما ان يكون زوجاً هو الحكم على فردية العدد بمعاندة الزوجية ولاشك في تغاير هذين المفهومين فان المفهوم من معاندة هذا لذاك غير المفهوم من معاندة ذاك لهذا. قلت نعم ولكن يرجع محصل المفهومين الى شيء واحد و هو المعاندة بين الشيئين فلافايدة يعتد بها في انعكاسها فلذا حكم القوم بان المنفصلة لاعكس لها اى العكس المعتد به فتأمل. ثم ليس المراد من تبديل الموضوع و المحمول تبديل الذات التي هي الموضوع فى الحقيقة و الوصف الذي هو المحمول بل المراد تبديل عنوانها.
His statement: 'whether the two terms...' The purpose of this generalization is that simple conversion applies to both categoricals and conditionals and is not specific to categoricals, as some appear to think, who defined it as the substitution of both the subject and predicate. There remains something here: if by 'the two terms' is meant the two terms of the proposition in reality, then none of the conversion of categoricals enters the definition, because the two terms in reality in categoricals are the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate, and in conversion the essence of the subject does not become the predicate nor the description of the subject the subject; rather, the description of the subject becomes the predicate and the essence of the predicate becomes the subject, as is apparent. And if by 'the two terms' is meant the two terms in mention, then exclusive disjunctions would have a converse, because their two terms, even if not distinct by nature, are distinct in mention, yet the scholars have stated that they have no converse. The answer, after conceding the second horn, is that what is meant by substitution is the meaningful substitution that changes the meaning, and there is no doubt that this meaning is not obtained in exclusive disjunctions, because it is apparent that the meaning of the exclusive disjunction does not change by substitution, since its meaning is the mutual opposition between the two things, whether its two terms are switched or not. If you say: We do not concede the non-change of meaning in the exclusive disjunction by switching the two terms, because it is apparent that the meaning of our statement: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' is the judgment that the number's evenness is mutually opposed to oddness, and the meaning of our statement: 'Either the number is odd or it is even' is the judgment that the number's oddness is mutually opposed to evenness, and there is no doubt that these two meanings are different, for the meaning of the opposition of this to that is not the same as the meaning of the opposition of that to this. I say: Yes, but the upshot of the two meanings returns to one thing, namely the mutual opposition between the two things, so there is no benefit worth considering in their conversion; therefore the scholars judged that the exclusive disjunction has no converse — i.e., no converse worth considering. So reflect. Then what is meant by the substitution of the subject and predicate is not the substitution of the essence that is the subject in reality and the description that is the predicate; rather, it is the substitution of their title.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical expansion of the definition of 'aks mustawi (simple conversion). The author begins by noting that the definition (given by the original text) generalizes to both categorical and conditional propositions, contrary to some who restrict it to categoricals. He then identifies a problem: if 'the two terms' refers to the real terms (essence of subject, description of predicate), then categorical conversion would not be covered because in conversion the essence of the subject does not become the predicate nor the description of the subject the subject; rather, the description of the subject becomes the predicate and the essence of the predicate becomes the subject. If instead 'the two terms' refers to the terms as mentioned, then exclusive disjunctions would have a converse, since their terms are distinct in mention, but the scholars (al-qawm) explicitly say they have no converse. The author resolves this by conceding the second interpretation and clarifying that substitution must be meaningful, i.e., it changes the meaning of the proposition. In exclusive disjunctions, switching the terms does not change the meaning because the core is opposition between the two things; thus there is no benefit to such conversion. An objection is raised: switching the terms does change the meaning because the emphasis shifts from one term to the other. The response: the upshot of both meanings is the same mutual opposition, so no real benefit. Hence the scholars say exclusive disjunctions have no converse worth considering. Finally, the author refines the definition: substitution is not of the real essence and description but of their 'unwan (title/indication). This ensures the definition covers all valid conversions.

Key Terms
العكس المستوى
المنفصلة
تبديل العنوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because switching the terms changes the meaning of the proposition.
B) Because the two terms are not distinct in mention.
C) Because the upshot of the meaning does not change, so there is no benefit.
D) Because exclusive disjunctions are not considered propositions.

Key Takeaway
The resolution of the dilemma shows that simple conversion requires a meaning change, and exclusive disjunctions fail this, leading to the refined definition of substitution of titles.

Distinguishing the Two Meanings of 'Aks: Act vs. Result
Commentator distinguishes real (act) and figurative (proposition) senses of 'aks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the real (masdari) and figurative (majazi) meanings of 'aks in logical texts and explain how they relate.

Core Idea
'Aks means both the act of conversion and the resulting proposition; confusing them leads to apparent contradictions.

The term 'aks (conversion) appears in logic books both as an operation and as its product. This distinction is crucial for understanding definitions and usages. In this passage, the commentator resolves a potential contradiction by clarifying these two senses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the potential confusion
Notice that defining 'aks as 'substitution of terms' seems to conflict with expressions like 'the particular affirmative is the converse of the universal affirmative.' In the latter, 'aks does not mean the act of substituting but rather the resulting proposition.
2
Recognize the real meaning
The real (haqiqi) meaning of 'aks is the infinitive (masdari) meaning the act of converting a proposition by swapping its subject and predicate. This is the technical term used among logicians.
The original text defines 'aks in this sense.
3
Recognize the figurative meaning
The figurative (majazi) meaning is the proposition that results from the act of conversion—i.e., the converse proposition itself.
For example, 'the particular affirmative is the converse of the universal affirmative' uses 'aks in this sense.
4
Understand the commentator's resolution
The commentator explains that the definition of 'aks as substitution applies to the real meaning, while the common expressions refer to the figurative meaning. They are not contradictory because they refer to different senses.
5
Examine the definition of the figurative meaning
The figurative 'aks is defined as 'the most specific proposition that is necessary for the original proposition by way of substitution, agreeing with it in quality and truth.' This definition includes genus (proposition) and differentia (specific necessity, agreement in quality and truth).
This definition ensures that the converse is uniquely determined and matches the original in affirmative/negative form and truth value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : و أعلم ان العكس كما يطلق .... الغرض من هذا الكلام دفع ما يتوهم في المقام ان هذا اعنى تعريف العكس بالتبديل ينا في ما وقع فى كتبهم من ان الموجبة الجزئية، عكس الموجبة الكلية والسالبة الكلية، عكس السالبة الكلية و غير هما فى الصور الجزئية لظهور ان ليس العكس فيها بمعنى التبديل. و حاصل الدفع : ان العكس المعرف بالتبديل غير العكس الذى وقع في عباراتهم فان الاول مستعمل في معناه الحقيقى اعنى المعنى المصدرى و الثانى فى معناه المجازي اعنى القضية الحاصلة من التبديل و الاول هو المصطلح فيما بينهم ولذا تصدى المصنف بتعريفه ويعرف العكس بالمعنى الثاني بانه اخص قضية لازمة للقضية بطريق التبديل موافقة لها في الكيف والصدق . و لا يذهب عليك : انه يمكن ان يكون قول المصنف تعريفاً للعكس بالمعنى الثاني يجعل المصدر على معنى المفعول اى العكس المستوى مبدل طرفى القضية او يجعله من باب اقامة السبب مقام المسبب اى: هو المبدل الحاصل بسبب تبديل طرفي القضية فافهم لكنه خلاف ظاهر عبارة المصنف وتصريح بعضهم.
His statement: 'And know that 'aks, as it is used...' The purpose of this speech is to repel a possible misunderstanding in this context, namely that this — i.e., the definition of 'aks as substitution — contradicts what occurs in their books, such as 'the particular affirmative is the converse of the universal affirmative' and 'the universal negative is the converse of the universal negative' and other particular forms, because it is apparent that 'aks here is not in the sense of substitution. The gist of the repulsion is that the 'aks defined by substitution is not the same as the 'aks that occurs in their expressions; the first is used in its real meaning, i.e., the infinitive meaning, and the second in its figurative meaning, i.e., the proposition resulting from substitution. The first is the technical term among them, hence the author undertook its definition. And the 'aks in the second sense is defined as 'the most specific proposition that is necessary for the proposition by way of substitution, agreeing with it in quality and truth.' And it should not escape you that it is possible that the author's statement is a definition of 'aks in the second sense by taking the infinitive in the sense of the passive participle — i.e., simple conversion is the substituted terms of the proposition — or by making it a case of substituting the cause for the effect — i.e., it is the substituted object resulting from the substitution of the two terms. So understand, but this is contrary to the apparent meaning of the author's statement and the explicit statement of some scholars.

Commentary
This passage clarifies a potential confusion about the definition of 'aks mustawi. The author notes that some might think the definition of 'aks as substitution contradicts common usage in logic books, e.g., 'the particular affirmative is the converse of the universal affirmative,' because in such statements 'aks does not mean the act of substitution but rather the resulting proposition. The author resolves this by distinguishing two meanings: the real (haqiqi) meaning, which is the infinitive (masdari) meaning the act of conversion, and the figurative (majazi) meaning, which is the proposition that results from substitution. The technical term among logicians is the first meaning, so the author (the original matn writer) defines that. The second meaning is defined as 'the most specific proposition that is necessary for the original proposition by way of substitution, agreeing in quality and truth.' The commentator then offers two possible interpretations of the author's wording: either the infinitive is used in the sense of the passive participle (i.e., 'conversion' means 'what is converted'), or it is a metonymy where the cause stands for the effect (i.e., the act of conversion stands for the converted proposition). He notes that these interpretations, though possible, contradict the apparent meaning of the author's words and the explicit statements of some scholars.

Resolving the Objection: Preservation of Truth in 'Aks Mustawi
Author refutes objection concerning false conversions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual truth and conditional truth preservation in the definition of simple conversion ('aks mustawi).

Core Idea
'Preservation of truth' means 'if true, then true', not 'both are true'.

The definition of simple conversion ('aks mustawi) requires that the converse preserve the truth of the original. But does this definition cover conversions of false propositions? An objection arises that it does not, because false propositions have false converses. The author clarifies that 'preservation of truth' is not about actual truth but about a conditional implication: if the original is true, then the converse is true. This ensures the definition applies universally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Someone might think the definition of 'aks mustawi requires the original and converse to both be true. This would exclude false propositions, since their converses are also false.
The objection is based on a literal reading of 'preservation of truth'.
2
Locate the clarification
The author states that 'we do not mean by preservation of truth that the original and the converse must be true in actuality.' Highlight this key phrase.
3
Interpret 'preservation of truth'
Understand that preservation of truth is a conditional: if the original is true, then the converse is true. It does not require actual truth of either proposition.
This is a standard interpretation in later logic.
4
Apply to false propositions
Even if the original is false, the conversion rule is still valid because the conditional holds. For example, 'All birds are mammals' (false) converts to 'Some mammals are birds' (false) – the conditional 'if true then true' is vacuously true.
This shows the definition covers all propositions.
5
Confirm coverage
The definition of 'aks mustawi now applies universally, regardless of truth value, because it only asserts a logical implication.

The author (probably Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) clarifies that 'preservation of truth' does not mean actual truth, but rather a hypothetical condition: if the original were true, the converse would also be true. In other words, the conversion rule holds for false propositions as well in terms of logical implication, not actual truth value. This ensures the definition covers all propositions, true or false, as long as the conditional implication is maintained.
