The Conditional Interpretation of Preservation of Truth in Conversion
Author addresses the objection that the definition excludes false propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual truth and conditional preservation of truth in the definition of simple conversion and apply this distinction to evaluate whether any given proposition qualifies for valid conversion.

Core Idea
Preservation of truth in conversion is a hypothetical necessity, not actual truth.

This passage addresses a common misunderstanding about the definition of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi). Some may think that 'preservation of truth' means both original and converse must be actually true, which would exclude false propositions. The commentator clarifies that it is a conditional: if the original were true, the converse would be true. This nuanced understanding prevents misapplication of conversion in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Notice the misunderstanding that the definition of simple conversion does not apply to false propositions because it requires 'preservation of truth'. This is the prima facie objection the author addresses.
2
Locate the key term 'preservation of truth'
Find the phrase baqa' al-sidq in the definition. The commentator specifies that this does not mean actual truth of both propositions.
3
Understand the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that 'preservation of truth' is a conditional: if the original proposition were assumed true, the truth of the converse would necessarily follow. It does not require that the original or converse be true in reality.
This is a shift from an actualist interpretation to a hypothetical one.
4
Apply to an example
Test a false proposition, e.g., 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause'. Its converse 'Everything that has a cause is temporally originated' is false. Determine if the conditional holds: if the original were true, would the converse be true? In this case, no, so the conversion is invalid. This demonstrates how the conditional test works.
The example is from Kalam; the original is actually true, but the converse is false, showing that conversion does not preserve truth in both directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بمعنى ان الأصل ...» اشارة الى دفع ما يتوهم من ان تعريف العكس المستوى على ما ذكره المصنف غير شامل على عكوس القضاياء الكاذبة كما هو ظاهر. و حاصله انا لا نعنى من بقاء الصدق انه يجب ان يكون الاصل والعكس صادقين في نفس الأمر في العكس المستوى بل المراد ان الاصل لو فرض صدقه لزم من صدقه صدق العكس و ان كان كاذباً في الحقيقة، هذا.
His statement: 'meaning that the original...' is an indication to refute the misunderstanding that the definition of simple conversion according to what the author mentioned does not include the converses of false propositions, as is apparent. Its summary is: we do not mean by 'preservation of truth' that it is necessary that the original and the converse be actually true in simple conversion. Rather, the meaning is that if the original were assumed true, the truth of the converse would necessarily follow from its truth, even if it is actually false. That's it.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification. It addresses a potential objection: the definition of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) might seem to exclude false propositions because it mentions 'preservation of truth'. The author explains that 'preservation of truth' does not mean both original and converse are actually true in reality. Rather, it means a conditional: if the original were true, then the converse would necessarily be true. This is a hypothetical necessity, not an actual one. Thus, a false proposition can have a valid simple conversion as long as the conditional holds. For example, 'Every animal is a human' is false, but its simple conversion 'Every human is an animal' is true; the conditional 'if every animal is human, then every human is animal' does not hold, so it is not a valid simple conversion. This nuanced understanding is crucial for correctly applying the rule of conversion.

The definition of simple conversion requires that if the original were true, the converse would be true, not that both are actually true.

The Essential Condition for Valid Simple Conversion
Author refutes objection that definitions cover coincidentally true pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from accidental truth preservation in determining whether a conversion is valid.

Core Idea
Conversion is valid only when truth follows by essence, not by accident of material.

In this passage, the author refutes an objection that would allow accidental pairs to count as converses. Understanding the distinction between essential and accidental truth preservation is crucial for correctly applying conversion in logical and theological arguments. The key is that 'preservation of truth' must be 'by essence' (min haythu al-dhat), meaning the truth of the converse follows necessarily from the truth of the original due to logical form, not due to specific subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that the definition of simple conversion would include pairs like 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is human' that are true together by accident. Recognize that this would make the condition too broad.
The objector uses examples where the terms are co-extensive.
2
Understand the response: truth by essence
The author says 'preservation of truth' must be 'by essence' (dhat), meaning the truth of the converse follows necessarily from the truth of the original due to the logical form, not due to the particular subject matter.
The term 'min haythu al-dhat' is key; it excludes accidental material coincidence.
3
Test with a counterexample
Consider 'Every human is animal' (true) and its converse 'Every animal is human' (false). The truth of the original does not guarantee the truth of the converse by essence, showing the necessity of the condition.
This example demonstrates that accidental truth in other pairs does not satisfy the condition.
4
Recognize accidental truth
The truth in the objection's examples is due to the particular materials (e.g., 'human' and 'rational' happen to have the same extension), not due to universality.
If the terms were different, the truth would not follow.
5
Note the asymmetry: truth preservation only
Only preservation of truth is required, not falsehood. The converse may be true even if the original is false (e.g., 'Every animal is human' false, 'Some human is animal' true).
This is because the converse is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the original, and a necessary consequence can be more general.

و قد اوردهنا ان هذا التعريف يصدق على القضاياء الصادقة مع الاصل بحسب الاتفاق كقولنا : كل انسان ناطق فانه يصدق مع قولنا كل ناطق انسان مع انه ليس عكسه و كقولنا كل انسان بشر فأنه يصدق مع قولنا كل بشر انسان مع انه ليس عكساً له. والجواب ان المراد من بقاء الصدق ان يكون من حيث الذات اى من غير نظر الى امر خارج و لاشك انه لا يلزم فى المثالين المذكورين ونحوهما من صدق الأصل صدق العكس نظراً الى ذواتها لجواز المحمول. الا ترى انه لا يصدق قولنا كل حيوان انسان مع انه يصدق قولنا كل انسان حیوان؟ و ما يترأى فى المثالين المذكورين ونحوهما من التصادق فانما هو من حيث خصوص المواد لا من حيث هو. بقى هنا شيء و هو ان المعتبر فى العكس المستوى انما هو بقاء الصدق، وبقاء الكذب ليس بلازم و ذلك ، لان العكس لازم للقضية فجاز ان يكون صادقاً مع كذبها كما ترى في قولنا كل حيوان انسان و بعض الانسان حيوان ضرورة ان الاول كاذب و الثانى صادق (الجواز ان يكون اللازم اعم من الملزوم ) كما تقرر في موضعه . (محمد علی)
And it has been objected here that this definition applies to propositions that happen to be true together with the original by coincidence, like our statement 'Every human is rational,' for it is true together with our statement 'Every rational is human,' even though it is not its converse; and like our statement 'Every human is a mortal,' for it is true together with our statement 'Every mortal is human,' even though it is not its converse. The answer is: what is meant by 'preservation of truth' is that it be by essence (dhat), i.e., without regard to an external matter. There is no doubt that in the two mentioned examples and similar ones, the truth of the converse does not follow from the truth of the original when considering their essences, because of the possibility of the predicate. Do you not see that our statement 'Every animal is human' is not true, while our statement 'Every human is animal' is true? And what appears in the two mentioned examples and similar ones of mutual truth is only due to the particular materials, not due to universality. There remains something here: what is considered in simple conversion is only preservation of truth; preservation of falsehood is not necessary. This is because the converse is a necessary consequence of the proposition, so it is possible that it is true while the original is false, as you see in our statement 'Every animal is human' and 'Some human is animal' – necessarily, the first is false and the second is true. (For it is possible that the necessary consequence is more general than the antecedent) as established in its place. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of an objection. The objection states that the definition of simple conversion would wrongly include pairs of propositions that are true together by accident, such as 'Every human is rational' and its apparent converse 'Every rational is human'. These are not true converses because the propositions are equivalent (co-extensive), but the conversion rule requires a necessary following from the original, not just accidental truth. The answer explains that 'preservation of truth' must be by essence (dhat), meaning that the truth of the converse follows from the truth of the original intrinsically, not due to the particular subject matter (material). For example, 'Every human is an animal' is true, but its converse 'Every animal is human' is false, showing that the truth does not follow by essence. The accidental truth in the examples is due to the specific materials (e.g., 'human' and 'rational' happen to have the same extension). The passage then adds a note: only preservation of truth is required, not preservation of falsehood. The converse may be true even if the original is false, because the necessary consequence (lazim) can be more general than the antecedent (malzum). An example: 'Every animal is human' is false, but its converse 'Some human is animal' is true. This highlights that the condition is one-way: truth of original implies truth of converse, but not vice versa.

Why Disjunctive Conditionals Lack Beneficial Conversion
Author explains conversion rules for conditional propositions and why disjunctives lack conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a conditional proposition can be converted and explain why disjunctives are excluded from standard conversion in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Conversion of conditionals is only recognized when it serves a logical purpose; disjunctives fail this test.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the logicians' rule that conversion applies to conjunctive conditionals (muttasil) but not to disjunctives (munfasil). We will unpack why a simple swap of parts does not count as conversion if it yields no new information or benefit. This nuance is key to understanding immediate inference in the Arabic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between muttasil and munfasil
Recall that a conjunctive conditional (muttasil) has the form 'If P then Q', while a disjunctive conditional (munfasil) has the form 'Either P or Q' (exclusive). Identify which type is being discussed in the passage.
2
Review the definition of conversion
Conversion (عكس) is defined as swapping the two parts (terms) of a proposition. The passage notes that substitution of titles is the core operation. Check the definition provided by the commentator.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objection states that swapping parts is conceivable in disjunctives too. The response agrees but claims it is not beneficial. Notice how the commentator uses the example of 'even/odd' to show that the concept changes, but logicians disregard it.
4
Understand why 'inconceivable' means 'not beneficial'
The passage clarifies that 'inconceivable' does not mean logically impossible; it means that logicians do not consider such conversion because it yields no useful inferential result. This is a pragmatic constraint on logical operations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اى الشرطيات المتصلة و أما الشرطيات المنفصلة فلا يتصور فيها العكس كما ذكره القوم لعدم امتياز جزئيها بحسب الطبع. فان قلت ان المراد من التبديل في تعريف العكس هو تبديل عنوان الطرفين ولاشك في أن ذلك متصور في الشرطيات المنفصلة و ان لم يتميز طرفا ها بحسب الطبع. :قلت لاريب فى ان المنفصلة عكساً فان المفهوم من قولنا اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً واما ان يكون فرداً غير المفهوم من قولنا اما ان يكون العدد فرداً او زوجاً لان المفهوم من معاندة هذا لذلك غير المفهوم من معاندة ذاك لهذا لكن لما لم يكن فيه فايدة لم يعتبروه وهذا هو المراد من عدم تصور العكس فيها. (عبد الرحيم)
That is, conditional conjunctives. As for disjunctive conditionals, conversion is not conceivable in them as the logicians have mentioned, because their two parts are not distinct by nature (tab'). If you say: 'What is meant by substitution in the definition of conversion is swapping the titles of the two extremes, and there is no doubt that this is conceivable in disjunctive conditionals, even if their two parts are not distinguished by nature.' I say: There is no doubt that the disjunctive has a conversion. For the concept of our statement 'Either the number is even or it is odd' is not the same as the concept of our statement 'Either the number is odd or it is even', because the concept of one contradicting the other is not the same as the concept of the other contradicting this one. But since there is no benefit in it, they did not consider it. This is what is meant by the inconceivability of conversion in them. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the conversion of conditionals. It states that conditional conjunctive propositions (muttasil, i.e., 'if...then') can be converted, but disjunctive conditionals (munfasil, i.e., 'either...or') cannot, according to the logicians. The reason given is that the two parts of a disjunctive are not naturally distinct in order; swapping them does not produce a new proposition that is logically different in a beneficial way. An objection is raised: the definition of conversion only requires swapping the terms, which is physically possible in disjunctives. The response acknowledges that a conceptual conversion exists: 'Either A or B' is not identical to 'Either B or A' because the sense of opposition is reversed. However, since there is no logical benefit (i.e., it does not yield a new inference or help in syllogisms), logicians disregard it. Thus, 'inconceivability' here means not beneficial, not literally impossible. This nuance is important for understanding the scope of conversion in the broader logical system.

Key Terms

الشرطيات المنفصلة

Key Takeaway
Conversion of conditionals is only meaningful when it yields logical benefit; disjunctives are excluded because swapping parts does not advance reasoning.

Simple Conversion and the Condition of Essential Truth
Definition of simple conversion and defense against objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given pair of propositions satisfies simple conversion by checking if truth is preserved essentially, not accidentally.

Core Idea
Truth must be preserved by essence, not by material coincidence.

Simple conversion is a fundamental operation in immediate inference. But not every true pair that looks like a conversion qualifies. The author defines it precisely and addresses a common objection: what about propositions that happen to be true together? The answer draws a critical distinction between essential and accidental truth preservation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of simple conversion
The passage defines simple conversion as: if the original (original proposition) is assumed true, then the truth of the converse necessarily follows. Note: the original may be actually false, but we consider the conditional.
This is the core condition — truth preservation under the assumption of truth.
2
Examine the objection about accidental coincidence
The objection gives examples like 'Every human is a speaker' and 'Every speaker is human' — both are true, but the latter is not the converse of the former because the terms are coextensive. The definition would wrongly include such cases.
The objection shows that mere mutual truth is not enough.
3
Grasp the answer: 'by essence' (min haythu al-dhat)
The author clarifies that truth must be preserved by essence, meaning due to the logical form and not due to particular material facts. In the examples, the mutual truth arises from the specific terms, not from the conversion rule.
The counterexample 'Every animal is a human' (false) vs 'Every human is an animal' (true) illustrates the point.
4
Apply the condition to test conversion validity
When checking a potential conversion, ask: Is it necessary that whenever the original is true, the converse is true, considering the logical form alone? If yes, the conversion is valid. If the truth depends on the specific subject matter, it is not.
This guards against confusing accidental coincidence with logical entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في العكس المستوى بل المراد ان الاصل لو فرض صدقه لزم من صدقه صدق العكس و ان كان كاذباً في الحقيقة، هذا. و قد اوردهنا ان هذا التعريف يصدق على القضاياء الصادقة مع الاصل بحسب الاتفاق كقولنا : كل انسان ناطق فانه يصدق مع قولنا كل ناطق انسان مع انه ليس عكسه و كقولنا كل انسان بشر فأنه يصدق مع قولنا كل بشر انسان مع انه ليس عكساً له. والجواب ان المراد من بقاء الصدق ان يكون من حيث الذات اى من غير نظر الى امر خارج و لاشك انه لا يلزم فى المثالين المذكورين ونحوهما من صدق الأصل صدق العكس نظراً الى ذواتها لجواز المحمول. الا ترى انه لا يصدق قولنا كل حيوان انسان مع انه يصدق قولنا كل انسان حیوان؟ و ما يترأى فى المثالين المذكورين ونحوهما من التصادق فانما هو من حيث خصوص المواد لا من حيث هو عموم.
Regarding simple conversion, the intended meaning is that if the original is assumed true, then from its truth the truth of the converse necessarily follows, even if it is actually false. This is the definition. And an objection was raised here: this definition applies to propositions that are true along with the original by coincidence, like our saying 'Every human is a speaker'—for it is true along with our saying 'Every speaker is human' while it is not its converse, and like our saying 'Every human is a mortal'—for it is true along with our saying 'Every mortal is human' while it is not its converse. The answer is that what is meant by preservation of truth is that it be by essence, i.e., without regard to an external matter. There is no doubt that in the two mentioned examples and their like, the truth of the original does not necessitate the truth of the converse with respect to their essences because of the possibility of the predicate. Do you not see that our saying 'Every animal is a human' is not true while our saying 'Every human is an animal' is true? And what appears in the two mentioned examples and their like of mutual truth is only by virtue of the particular material, not by virtue of what is universal.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and defends it against an objection. The definition states that if the original proposition is true, the converse must also be true (even if the original is actually false). The objection points to examples like 'Every human is a speaker' — this is true, and its supposed converse 'Every speaker is human' is also true, but it is not a valid conversion because the terms are coextensive. The answer clarifies that truth must be preserved 'by essence' (min haythu al-dhat), not by accidental material coincidence. The author gives a counterexample: 'Every animal is a human' is false, while 'Every human is an animal' is true, showing that accidental truth is not the criterion. The exchange guards against confusing logical conversion with coincidental mutual truth in specific materials.

Conversion of Affirmative Propositions: Truth Preservation Rule
Author clarifies conversion rule: truth preservation only, and affirmatives convert to particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for affirmative propositions (universal and particular) and explain why falsehood is not preserved.

Core Idea
Conversion preserves truth, not falsehood; affirmatives always convert to particular affirmative.

In this lesson, we examine a key rule of immediate inference: conversion ('aks). The passage clarifies that conversion only requires that if the original is true, the converse is true; falsehood does not transfer. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly transforming propositions in logical arguments within Islamic sciences, such as in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Determine its quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative). For example, 'Every human is an animal' is universal affirmative (A).
2
Swap subject and predicate
Exchange the subject and predicate terms to form the converse. For 'Every human is an animal', the converse is 'Every animal is a human' (which is false). But the rule requires truth preservation, not falsehood.
3
Apply the conversion rule for affirmatives
All affirmatives (A and I) convert to particular affirmative (I). So the correct converse of 'Every human is an animal' is 'Some animal is a human'.
This rule ensures regularity, even though in some materials (coextensive terms) the universal could convert to universal.
4
Verify truth preservation
Check that if the original is true, the converse is also true. The passage shows that falsehood does not need to be preserved; a false original may have a true converse.
Example: 'Every animal is a human' is false, but its converse 'Some human is an animal' is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى هنا شيء و هو ان المعتبر فى العكس المستوى انما هو بقاء الصدق، وبقاء الكذب ليس بلازم و ذلك ، لان العكس لازم للقضية فجاز ان يكون صادقاً مع كذبها كما ترى في قولنا كل حيوان انسان و بعض الانسان حيوان ضرورة ان الاول كاذب و الثانى (صادق الجواز ان يكون اللازم اعم من الملزوم ) كما تقرر في موضعه . (محمد علی) (۵) اى قضية موجبة و كذا قوله كان العكس موجبة اى قضية موجبة و كذلك قوله : «و ان كان سالبة، كان العكس سالبة فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی) (۶) اذلولم يكن كذلك ، لا يلزم صدق العكس من صدق الاصل. (عبد الرحيم) (۷) اى سواء كانت القضية كلية او جزئية، كقولنا كل انسان حیوان و بعض الانسان حيوان و بعد التبديل يكون الحيوان موضوعاً و الانسان محمولاً ولا صدق الانسان على الحيوان كلياً يصح لاستحالة صدق الاخص على كل فرد من افراد الاعم. وقوله: قد يكون اعم» اشارة الى ان ذلك في بعض المواد لا في جمعيها لجواز المساواة فى بعضها في الكلية والجزئية وجواز العكس في بعض مواد الموجبة الجزئية لكن لما لم يكن ذلك مطرداً حكموا بان الموجبة كلية كانت او جزئية لا تنعكس الا الى الموجبة الجزئية ليصح الحكم فى الكل بخلاف الايجاب الكلى فانه لا يصح الا فى بعض المواد. ميرزا محمد علی)
There remains a point: what is considered in simple conversion is only preservation of truth; preservation of falsehood is not necessary. That is because the converse is a necessary consequent of the proposition, so it is possible that it be true while the original is false, as you see in our saying 'Every animal is a human' and 'Some human is an animal' — necessarily the first is false and the second is true (it is possible that the necessary consequent be more general than the necessitating thing) as established in its place. (Muhammad 'Ali) (5) i.e., an affirmative proposition, and likewise his saying 'the converse is affirmative' means an affirmative proposition, and likewise his saying 'and if it is negative, the converse is negative' so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) (6) Because if it were not so, the truth of the converse would not follow from the truth of the original. ('Abd al-Rahim) (7) i.e., whether the proposition is universal or particular, like our saying 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some human is an animal' — after transposition, 'animal' becomes subject and 'human' predicate, and the truth of 'human' being said of 'animal' universally is not valid because of the impossibility of the more specific being true of every individual of the more general. And his saying 'it may be more general' indicates that this is in some materials, not all, because equality is possible in some in universality and particularity, and conversion is possible in some materials of the particular affirmative. But since it is not regular, they ruled that the affirmative, whether universal or particular, converts only to the particular affirmative so that the ruling holds for all, unlike the universal affirmative which is valid only in some materials. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage addresses a remaining point: the condition for simple conversion is only that truth is preserved, not that falsehood is preserved. The author explains that the converse is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the original, so it can be true even when the original is false. An example: 'Every animal is a human' is false, but its converse 'Some human is an animal' is true. This shows that falsehood does not transfer. The glosses clarify that the rule for affirmative propositions (whether universal or particular) is that they convert to particular affirmative (I). This is because the predicate of the original becomes the subject of the converse, and the more specific cannot be predicated universally of the more general. However, in some materials where terms are coextensive, universal affirmative could convert to universal affirmative, but since that is not regular, the logicians adopted the rule that all affirmatives convert to particular affirmative for uniformity.

Conversion of Disjunctive Conditionals: Possibility vs Benefit
Author defends the standard view on conversion of disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the classical logicians did not recognize conversion for disjunctive conditionals and identify the role of epistemic benefit in defining logical operations.

Core Idea
Conversion of disjunctives is possible in form but useless in practice.

This passage addresses a subtle point: can disjunctive conditionals be converted? The standard claim says no, because the two parts lack natural order. An objector argues that swapping the titles is always possible. The author concedes that a kind of conversion exists, but it is ignored because it yields no new information. Understanding this helps you see how pragmatic considerations shape logical rules.

Guided Path
1
Understand the standard view
The logicians claim that conversion is not conceivable for disjunctive conditionals because the two parts are not naturally distinct (they have no inherent priority). This is the default position.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says that conversion only requires swapping the titles (i.e., exchanging the two disjuncts), which is always possible regardless of natural distinction. So conversion should be conceivable.
3
Analyse the answer
The author agrees that swapping produces a different meaning (the relation of opposition changes direction), so there is a formal converse. However, it is not counted as conversion because it provides no new information (no fa'ida).
The example: 'Either the number is even or odd' vs 'Either the number is odd or even' – the meaning differs but is trivial.
4
Connect to broader principle
Logical operations like conversion are recognized only when they yield epistemic benefit. This pragmatic criterion prevents the list of valid inferences from being cluttered with trivial permutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸) اى الشرطيات المتصلة و أما الشرطيات المنفصلة فلا يتصور فيها العكس كما ذكره القوم لعدم امتياز جزئيها بحسب الطبع. فان قلت ان المراد من التبديل في تعريف العكس هو تبديل عنوان الطرفين ولاشك في أن ذلك متصور في الشرطيات المنفصلة و ان لم يتميز طرفا ها بحسب الطبع. :قلت لاريب فى ان المنفصلة عكساً فان المفهوم من قولنا اما ان يكون العدد زوجاً واما ان يكون فرداً غير المفهوم من قولنا اما ان يكون العدد فرداً او زوجاً لان المفهوم من معاندة هذا لذلك غير المفهوم من معاندة ذاك لهذا لكن لما لم يكن فيه فايدة لم يعتبروه وهذا هو المراد من عدم تصور العكس فيها. (عبد الرحيم)
(8) i.e., the connected conditionals (mutasila). As for the disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), conversion is not conceivable in them as the scholars mentioned, due to the lack of natural distinction between their two parts. For if you say: what is meant by transposition in the definition of conversion is transposition of the titles of the two sides, and there is no doubt that this is conceivable in disjunctive conditionals even if their two sides are not naturally distinct. I say: there is no doubt that the disjunctive conditional has a converse, for the meaning of our saying 'Either the number is even or it is odd' is different from the meaning of our saying 'Either the number is odd or it is even', because the meaning of one being opposite to the other is different from the meaning of the other being opposite to the one. However, since there is no benefit (fa'ida) in it, they did not consider it, and this is the meaning of 'conversion is not conceivable' in them. ('Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about conversion in disjunctive conditionals (shartiyya munfasila). The standard view is that conversion is not applicable to disjunctives because the two parts are not naturally distinct in order (there is no natural priority). The objection argues that conversion only requires swapping the terms (titles), which is possible in disjunctives. The answer concedes that there is a kind of conversion: 'Either the number is even or odd' is not identical in meaning to 'Either the number is odd or even' because the relation of opposition is asymmetric. However, the logicians did not treat this as true conversion because it yields no new information (no fa'ida). The passage highlights the pragmatic dimension of logical operations: conversion is only considered when it produces epistemic benefit.

Immediate Inference
advanced
15 min
Three Methods for Proving Conversion of Propositions
Author enumerates and explains three methods for proving propositional conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three methods (supposition, conversion, and reductio) used by Islamic logicians to prove conversion rules, and evaluate the validity of the conversion method against the circularity objection.

Core Idea
Three methods prove conversion; only reductio escapes circularity by deriving a self-contradiction.

This passage from a logical commentary details the three methods logicians use to establish that propositions can be validly converted. You will learn each method, understand an important objection about circularity, and see how reductio ad absurdum provides a robust proof. These tools are essential for verifying immediate inferences in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three methods overview
The author lists three methods: supposition (iftirad), conversion (aks), and reductio (khalaf). Supposition is mentioned but not elaborated here; it involves assuming an individual instance. Conversion proves a converse by assuming its contradictory and deriving a contradiction. Reductio combines the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce a self-negation.
All three are used by logicians to establish conversion rules like 'Every A is B' implies 'Some B is A'.
2
Analyze the second method (aks)
To prove the converse of a proposition, assume the contradictory of the desired converse. For example, to prove 'Some animal is human' from 'Every human is animal', assume 'No animal is human'. Convert that to 'No human is animal', which contradicts the original. Hence the original implies the converse. The same works for universal negatives.
The conversion method relies on the rule that the contradictory of a converse converts to a proposition that contradicts the original.
3
Examine the circularity objection
An objector argues that this method involves circularity: proving the conversion of a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some animal is human') requires knowledge of the conversion of a universal negative (since the assumed contradictory is universal negative), and vice versa. The author replies that circularity only occurs if both are proved simultaneously; one can prove them separately without circularity.
This objection is a standard challenge; the response shows that the method is not inherently flawed.
4
Master the third method (khalaf)
This is the reductio method. To prove a converse, assume its contradictory and combine it with the original proposition in a first-figure categorical syllogism. The conclusion will be an impossibility (e.g., 'No human is human'). Since the original is true and the syllogistic form is valid, the impossibility must stem from the assumed contradictory, proving it false and the converse true.
The method is called 'khalaf' (reductio ad absurdum) and is considered more rigorous because it avoids the circularity of the second method.
5
Understand the necessity condition
A further refinement: the claim is that the original necessarily implies the converse (luzum), not merely that both happen to be true. The reductio method establishes this necessary implication by showing that assuming the contradictory leads to an impossibility. If the implication were merely coincidental, the contradictory could be true without contradiction.
This ensures the conversion rule is a logical necessity, not an accidental truth.

ارتفاع النقيضين. بان تجعله صغرى لا يجابه واصل القضية كبرى لكليته. لا يقال : ان السلب رفع الايجاب والايجاب لا يتصور بين الشيء ونفسه لان الكلام في القضاياء المتعارفة التي يراد من موضوعها الافراد ومن لانتفاء المحمول و ما استدل به لا ينتهض دليلاً لجواز ان يكون بعض افراد الموضوع موجوداً فيصدق الموجبة الجزئية الصغرى وبعضها غير موجود فيصدق النتيجة السالبة الجزئية. لانا نقول: لانسلم ذلك ، لان موضوع النتيجة هو الموضوع فى الصغرى فاذا ثبت ان موضوع الصغرى موجود فلا نقول بانتفاء الموضوع في النتيجة والا لاختلف موضوع الصغرى و موضوع النتيجة. قوله: «لان الاصل صادق يريد ان هيهنا ثلاثة اشياء أصل القضية ونقيض العكس و هيئة التأليف و هذا المجال لابد وان يكون ناشئاً عن احدها لاسبيل الى الأول لانه مفروض الصدق و لا الى الثالث لانه الشكل الاول و هو بين الانتاج فتعين ان يكون ناشئاً عن الثاني و هذا معنى قوله: منشأه هو نقيض العكس». يعني: فنضمه الى الاصل بان نجعله كبرى من الشكل الاول لكونه سلباً كلياً و الاصل صغرى لا يجابه فنقول : كل انسان حيوان بالضرورة او دائماً ودائماً لا شيء من الحيوان بانسان مادام حيواناً ينتج: لا شيء من الانسان بانسان بالضرورة او دائماً وهو باطل لاستحالة سلب الشيء عن نفسه. ثم نقول: هذا المحال لم ينشأ عن الاصل الذى هو الصغرى لانه مفروض الصدق ولا عن الهيئة لكونها منتجة فتعين ان ينشأ عن الكبرى التى هى نقيض العكس لانحصار الاجزاء فيها واذا كان النقيض مستلزماً للمحال كان محالا ، لان مستلزم المحال محال فاذا كان محالاً كان العكس حقاً لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين وهو المطلوب وكذا الكلام فى العامتين فلا نعيده هنا.
Know that the people argued in explaining the conversions of propositions by three methods: First: supposition, and the annotator will mention it at the end of the section on conversion of the contradictory. Second: conversion (al-aks), which is to convert the contradictory of the conversion and it returns to what is in the original. For example, it is said: If 'Every human is an animal' or 'Some human is an animal' is true, then 'Some animal is human' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'No animal is human', and it converts to our saying 'No human is animal', whereas the original judgment was 'Every human is an animal' or 'Some human is animal' — this is a contradiction. So in our case we say: Whenever 'No human is stone' is true, 'No stone is human' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'Some stone is human', and it converts to our saying 'Some human is stone', whereas the original judgment was 'No human is stone' — this is a contradiction. If you say: The argument by conversion is invalid because it entails circularity, for knowledge of the conversion of the particular affirmative depends on knowledge of the conversion of the universal negative, and knowledge of the conversion of the universal negative depends on knowledge of the conversion of the particular affirmative, as is clear to one who contemplates the two mentioned examples. I say: The necessity of circularity is only when the two arguments are combined, as is apparent, and that is prevented; necessarily, whoever explains conversion by this method for the particular affirmative does not explain conversion by it for the negatives, and whoever explains conversion by it for the negatives does not explain conversion by it for the particular affirmative. What appears in some books of mentioning both together is by way of preventing combination. Third: reductio (al-khalf), with a damma on the kha' and a sakin on the lam: combining the contradictory of the conversion with the original to produce from the first figure the negation of a thing of itself, as the annotator mentioned. And he objected here: If what they mean by their saying 'If some J is B is true then some B is J is true' is that this truth necessitates that truth, i.e., it is impossible for the truth of one to be separate from the truth of the other, then we do not accept that if it does not necessitate it then its contradictory is true, because it is possible for it to be true along with possibility of separation and lack of necessity; necessarily, the compound is negated by the negation of one of its parts as well. And if what is meant is the truth of the original, whether by way of necessity or coincidence, then we accept it but it does not benefit, even though the required thing—namely, the necessity—is true, because the general does not indicate the specific. And it was answered: We choose the first and say: By the truth of the contradictory is meant the possibility of its truth, and that is realized by the lack of necessity as well, because if the converse were not necessary for the original—i.e., inseparable from it—then it would be possible for it to be separate, so it would be possible for its contradictory to be true with it, and it would be possible for a thing to be vacant of the two contradictories; but the truth of its contradictory with it is impossible, and the possibility of the impossible is impossible. And in another expression: The claim is the necessity of the truth of the converse upon the truth of the original, otherwise it would be possible for its contradictory to be true with it, but that is impossible because it entails impossibility. That is because of the impossibility of the lifting of the two contradictories. You make it a minor premise in the affirmative and the original judgment as a major due to its universality. It is not said: Negation is the removal of affirmation, and affirmation is not conceivable between a thing and itself, because the discussion is about conventional propositions whose subject is intended as individuals and predicate as concept, and there is no doubt of their difference. Meaning: The negation of a thing from itself is impossible because it has been established that the affirmation of a thing for itself is necessary. It is not said: We do not accept its impossibility because it is possible to negate a thing from itself when it does not exist. We say: Even though the truth of a negative may be due to the non-existence of its subject or due to the non-existence of the predicate with the existence of the subject, here it is only due to the non-existence of the predicate, necessarily the subject exists here where the contradictory of the converse is assumed to be true, and it is the particular affirmative. It is not said: We do not accept that the truth of the negative here is due to the non-existence of the predicate, and what is adduced does not serve as proof because it is possible that some individuals of the subject exist so the particular affirmative minor premise is true, and some do not exist so the particular negative conclusion is true. We say: We do not accept that, because the subject of the conclusion is the subject in the minor premise; so if it is established that the subject of the minor premise exists, then we do not say that the subject in the conclusion is non-existent, otherwise the subject of the minor and the subject of the conclusion would differ. His saying: 'Because the original is true' means that here there are three things: the original judgment, the contradictory of the converse, and the form of composition; and this impossibility must arise from one of them. There is no way to the first because it is assumed true, nor to the third because it is the first figure which is evidently productive, so it must arise from the second. That is the meaning of his saying: 'Its source is the contradictory of the converse.' Meaning: We combine it with the original by making it a major premise in the first figure because it is a universal negative, and the original is a minor premise in the affirmative, so we say: 'Every human is animal necessarily or always' and 'Always no animal is human as long as it is animal' yields: 'No human is human necessarily or always', which is false because of the impossibility of negating a thing from itself. Then we say: This impossibility did not arise from the original which is the minor premise because it is assumed true, nor from the form because it is productive, so it must arise from the major which is the contradictory of the converse because the parts are limited to it. And if the contradictory entails impossibility, it is impossible, because that which entails impossibility is impossible. So if it is impossible, then the converse is true because of the impossibility of the lifting of the two contradictories, and that is what is required. And the same discussion applies to the two universals, so we do not repeat it here.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation by the annotator (Mirza Muhammad Ali) of the three methods logicians use to prove conversion rules for propositions. The first method is supposition (al-iftirad), which will be discussed later. The second method is conversion itself (al-aks): one takes the contradictory of the desired converse and shows that it would contradict the original. For example, if 'Every human is animal' is true, and we want to prove 'Some animal is human', we assume the opposite: 'No animal is human'. This converts to 'No human is animal', which contradicts the original. Thus the original implies the converse. The author gives a similar example for 'No human is stone'. A potential objection is raised: this method involves circularity because proving the conversion of particular affirmative depends on universal negative and vice versa. The reply is that circularity only occurs if both are proved simultaneously; one can prove them separately without circularity. The third method is reductio (al-khalf): combine the contradictory of the desired converse with the original to derive a self-negation via first-figure syllogism. For instance, to prove 'No stone is human' from 'No human is stone', assume the opposite 'Some stone is human', combine with original as major and minor. The result is a contradiction like 'No human is human', which is impossible. Since the impossibility cannot come from the original (true) or the syllogistic form (valid), it must come from the assumed contradictory, proving the converse. The author then addresses an objection about the necessity of implication: if the original does not necessitate the converse, it might still be true coincidentally. The reply clarifies that the claim is about necessary implication, not mere coincidence. The passage concludes with a standard reductio argument exemplified with 'human' and 'animal'. Throughout, the author stays true to the first-figure syllogistic form and the principle of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The reductio method is the most reliable for proving conversion because it avoids circularity by deriving an impossibility from the assumption of the contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conversion of Particular Negative: The Ancient Rule and Its Exception
Author explains that ancient logicians ruled that particular negative does not convert, except for specific conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of particular negative propositions can convert and explain why conditioned propositions are an exception.

Core Idea
The particular negative normally does not convert, but conditioned ones like 'Some J is not B as long as J' do.

This passage discusses a nuanced rule in immediate inference. While ancient logicians held that particular negative propositions generally do not convert, they recognized two exceptions: the particular conditioned and the particular customary. Understanding this exception is crucial for correctly handling conditional statements in Islamic logic, especially when temporal or customary conditions are attached to the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the first sentence: 'Know that the ancient logicians ruled absolutely that the particular negative does not convert.' This is the default position. Particular negative means 'Some J is not B' (e.g., 'Some Muslim is not sinful').
This rule is universally accepted for unconditioned particulars.
2
Recognize the exceptions
The passage says exceptions are 'the particular conditioned (al-mashruta al-khassa) and the particular customary (al-urfiyya al-khassa)'. These add a condition like 'as long as J' or 'by custom'. They convert 'like themselves', meaning the converse is also a conditioned particular negative.
The condition is attached to the subject: 'as long as J' in the example.
3
Walk through the example
The example: 'Some J is not B as long as J, not always' is true. This implies mutual exclusion (tanā'ī) of J and B on the essence of J: they cannot both be true of the same subject at the same time. Since the negation is not permanent, B must apply at some time, and then at another time it is negated. Thus, 'Some B is not J as long as B, not always' is also true.
The term 'tanā'ī' means mutual exclusion: J and B cannot coexist.
4
Understand the condition for validity
The final comment notes that this explanation fails if the original is purely particular (without condition). In that case, both original and converse are merely particular, and the argument about mutual exclusion and times does not apply. The conversion holds only when the proposition is conditioned.
The condition ensures that the subject's span is limited, allowing the temporal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم ان قدماء المنطقيين حكموا على الاطلاق ان السالبة الجزئية لا ينعكس و هو حق فيها عدا الخاصتين اما المشروطة الخاصة و العرفية الخاصة فانهما ينعكسان كانفسهما، مثلاً اذا صدق بعض ج ليس ب مادام ج لا دائماً اقتضى ذلك تنا في وصفى جوب الصادقين على ذات ج ووجود كل واحد من الوصفين فى وقت اما ج فلانه عنوان الموضوع و اما ب فلانا حكمنا بلا دوام السلب فيلزم ثبوت الايجاب و اذا تنا في تلك الذات لصدق كل واحد منها عليها صدق سلب كل واحد منها عنها في وقت آخر فاذا صدق الاصل صدق العكس فيصدق بعض ب ليس ج مادام ب لا دائماً و هو المطلوب و هذا العكس مما عثر عليه اثر الذئب للمفضل بن عمر الابهرى (جوهر النضيد) وذلك لما تقرر سابقاً من انه اذا صدق الاخص صدق الاعم. لا يخفى : ان هذا البيان لا يتم اذا كان الاصل جزئياً لان كلا الجزئين ح تكونان جزئيتين.
Know that the ancient logicians ruled absolutely that the particular negative does not convert, and that is true except for two specific propositions: the particular conditioned (al-mashruta al-khassa) and the particular customary (al-urfiyya al-khassa), for they convert like themselves. For example, if 'Some J is not B as long as J, not always' is true, that entails a mutual exclusion of two describers necessarily applying to the essence of J, and each of the two describers exists at a time; as for J, because it is the title of the subject; as for B, because we judge by the non-permanence of the negation, so affirmation is necessitated. And if those two describers mutually exclude being both true of that essence at the same time, then the negation of each of them from it is true at another time. So if the original is true, the converse is true, so 'Some B is not J as long as B, not always' is true, and that is what is required. And this conversion is one of the rare finds of al-Mufaddal ibn Umar al-Abhari (Jawhar al-Nadid). That is because it has been established previously that if the more specific is true, the more general is true. It is not hidden: this explanation does not hold if the original is particular, because then both propositions would be particular.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of particular negative propositions. The author states that ancient logicians generally held that the particular negative (e.g., 'Some J is not B') does not convert. However, there is an exception for two types of conditionals: the particular conditioned (al-mashruta al-khassa) and the particular customary (al-urfiyya al-khassa). These convert like themselves, meaning the converse is also a particular negative with the same conditions. The author gives an example: if 'Some J is not B as long as J (i.e., while it is J), not always' is true, then this implies that J and B are mutually exclusive describers on the essence of J. Since the negation is not permanent, there must be a time when J is B (affirmation). And because J and B cannot both be true of the same subject at the same time, they must be separate at different times. Therefore, 'Some B is not J as long as B, not always' is also true. The author attributes this rare insight to al-Abhari. The comment at the end notes that if the original 'Some J is not B' is purely particular (without conditions), the explanation does not hold because both original and converse would be merely particular, and the argument about mutual exclusion and times does not apply. This passage is an expansion on the standard rule, showing the nuance in the conversion of conditionals.

Key Terms
سالبة جزئية
مشروطة خاصة
عرفية خاصة
تنا

Key Takeaway
The particular negative normally does not convert, except when it is conditioned (temporally or customarily), in which case it converts to another conditioned particular negative.

Conversion of Particular Negatives for Special Conditionals
Commentary on conversion of particular negatives for special conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a particular negative proposition can be validly converted, specifically for special conditionals (mashruta khassa and 'urfiyya khassa).

Core Idea
Particular negatives normally don't convert, but special conditionals do.

The ancient logicians ruled that particular negative propositions generally do not convert. However, there are exceptions: propositions qualified by a special condition—such as 'as long as it is C'—can be converted like themselves. Understanding this exception is crucial for handling certain types of conditional statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Examine the proposition to determine its type: universal or particular, affirmative or negative. Look for any temporal or conditional qualifiers like 'as long as it is C' or 'always'.
The key is the presence of 'mādāma' (as long as) or similar phrase.
2
Check if it is a special conditional
If the proposition is a particular negative ('Some A is not B') but includes a condition like 'as long as it is A', it is a special conditional. There are two types: mashruta khassa and 'urfiyya khassa.
These convert like themselves, meaning the converse maintains the same form.
3
Apply the conversion rule
For a special conditional particular negative, if 'Some A is not B as long as it is A (but not always)' is true, then 'Some B is not A as long as it is B (but not always)' is also true. The reason is that the non-perpetual negation implies a conflict of descriptions leading to the converse.
This is proven by the logical conflict in the descriptions, as explained by al-Abhari.
4
Verify the converse
Check that the converse preserves the same truth conditions. The original's truth ensures the converse's truth because of the logical relations between the descriptions.
This method does not work for ordinary particular negatives without the special condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(17) Know that the ancient logicians ruled absolutely that the particular negative does not convert, and this is true for it except for the two special conditionals: the conditional special and the conventional special. For these two, they convert like themselves. For example, if it is true that 'Some C is not B as long as it is C, but not always', this entails a conflict in the two descriptions: necessity and truth apply to the essence of C, and each of the two descriptions exists at a time. For C is the title of the subject, and B is because we ruled that the negation is not perpetual, so affirmation is necessitated. And when those two descriptions conflict on that essence due to the truth of each on it, then the negation of each from it at another time is true. So if the original is true, the converse is true, so it is true that 'Some B is not C as long as it is B, but not always', which is what was required. This conversion was discovered by the effect of the wolf? for Mufaddal ibn Umar al-Abhari. (Jawhar al-Nadid) (18) And that is because it was established previously that if the more specific is true, the more general is true. (Muhammad Ali) (19) It is not hidden: This explanation is not complete if the original is particular, because both parts would be particular, and the particular does not produce in the major premise of the first figure unless the major is universal, as will come. It cannot be said: This is if we make the contradictory the minor and the first or second part the major, but if we make the contradictory the major, it is valid absolutely because it is absolutely universal. We say: Then another problem arises, which is that the minor would be negative, because the second part of the original must be negative, as is apparent. If it is said: We make first the first part of the original the minor and the contradictory the major, and second we make the contradictory the minor and the second part of the original the major, so no problem arises at all. We say: In the second also, the major would be particular, so the problem is not removed, because the whole argument is based on combining the contradictory with the first part of the original to produce a conclusion that conflicts with the result of combining it with the second part of the original. On what is mentioned, the result from combining it with the first part is, for example, 'Some writer is always a writer', and the result from combining it with the second part is, for example, 'Some finger-mover is not actually a finger-mover'. Where is the conflict between them? So if the original is particular, another method is necessary, namely hypothesis. For example, we assume in the mentioned example the essence that is true of 'writer' and 'finger-mover as long as it is a writer, but not always', e.g., Amr. So Amr is a finger-mover, which is apparent, and he is not actually a writer; otherwise he would always be a writer and thus always a finger-mover, because we ruled in the original that he is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, and he was a finger-mover not always. And if it is true of him that he is a finger-mover and not actually a writer, then it is true that 'Some finger-mover is not actually a writer', which is the concept of the non-perpetuality of the conversion as before. If the ruling were explained by this method, it would be better because it applies to both the universal and particular originals, as is not hidden. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of particular negative propositions. The commentator first states that ancient logicians held that the particular negative ('Some C is not B') generally does not convert (i.e., we cannot infer 'Some B is not C'). However, there are two exceptions: the conditional special and the conventional special. These are special types of propositions where the subject is restricted by a condition (e.g., 'as long as it is C') and the predicate is not permanent. The commentator explains that in such cases, the conversion holds, and gives an example: if 'Some C is not B as long as it is C (but not always)' is true, then 'Some B is not C as long as it is B (but not always)' is also true. This is because the non-perpetual negation implies a conflict in the descriptions, leading to the truth of the converse. The commentary notes that this discovery is attributed to Mufaddal ibn Umar al-Abhari. Then (18) adds a brief justification: the more specific implies the more general. In (19), a problem arises: the previous explanation does not hold when the original proposition is itself particular (i.e., 'Some C is not B' without temporal qualification). In that case, both parts of the original become particular, and the first-figure syllogism requires a universal major. The commentator considers and rejects several attempts to avoid this problem. He then provides an alternative method using hypothesis: assume a specific individual (e.g., Amr) that satisfies the conditions, and from that derive the conversion via particular facts. This method works for both universal and particular originals.

المشروطة الخاصة
العرفية الخاصة

Hypothetical Method for Converting Particular Negatives
Commentary on the hypothetical method for converting particular negatives when original is particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hypothetical method (iftiraḍ) to prove the conversion of a particular negative proposition when the standard syllogistic method fails due to the original being particular.

Core Idea
When syllogistic proof fails, assume a concrete individual to demonstrate the conversion.

The commentator shows that the previous proof for converting special conditionals does not work when the original proposition is itself particular (i.e., 'Some C is not B' without temporal qualification). This leads to a dead end because both premises become particular, invalidating the syllogism. The solution is to use hypothesis: assume a specific individual that satisfies the conditions and derive the converse from that individual's properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with particular originals
When the original proposition is 'Some C is not B' (a pure particular negative, not a special conditional), the standard proof fails because both parts of the original become particular. The first-figure syllogism requires a universal major premise.
The commentator mentions that if we make the contradictory (nāqiḍ) the major or minor, various problems arise, such as the minor becoming negative or the major remaining particular.
2
Understand the hypothetical method
Instead of a syllogistic proof, we assume a concrete individual that satisfies the conditions of the original proposition. For example, if the original is 'Some writer is not a finger-mover as long as he is a writer (not always)', we hypothesize a specific person, say Amr, who is that subject.
This individual must exemplify the truth of the original proposition.
3
Derive properties of the hypothesized individual
From the assumption, we deduce that Amr is a finger-mover (since he is a writer and as long as he is writer, he is a finger-mover) and that he is not actually a writer (if he were, he would be always a finger-mover, contradicting the 'not always').
This uses the definition of the special conditional: the negation is not perpetual.
4
Construct the converse from individual facts
Since Amr is a finger-mover and not actually a writer, it follows that 'Some finger-mover is not actually a writer'. This is the converse, with the understanding of non-perpetuality as before.
This method works for both universal and particular originals, and is more general.

The Reflection of General and Special Possible Propositions
Author examines reflection of possible propositions and identifies a hidden difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the reflection conditions of general possible (mumkina 'amma) and special possible (mumkina khassa) propositions, and identify why the special possible requires additional scrutiny.

Core Idea
Reflection of possible propositions is not uniform; special possible propositions present a hidden asymmetry.

The author begins by noting that the reflection of the general possible is straightforward, but the special possible is problematic. In this lesson, we will dissect the structure of both types and see why the special possible fails to automatically convert.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of possible propositions
Distinguish the general possible (mumkina 'amma), where both subject and predicate are possible with general possibility, from the special possible (mumkina khassa), where the subject is generally possible but the predicate is specially possible (i.e., actuality is not excluded).
The key difference is the modality of the predicate.
2
Examine the conversion of the general possible
In the general possible, both contracts (position and predication) are by general possibility restricted to existence, so converting subject and predicate yields an equivalent proposition.
No asymmetry; conversion is valid.
3
Analyze the special possible conversion
In the special possible, the subject has general possibility while the predicate has special possibility. The converse would require the predicate (now subject) to be qualified by the original subject with special possibility, which does not follow from the original.
The asymmetry makes the conversion invalid straightforwardly.

His statement: 'And its converse follows...' I say: This is apparent in the general possible (mumkina 'amma), because both contracts (position and predication) are by general possibility restricted to the side of existence in it. But in the special possible (mumkina khassa), there is hiddenness, because here the contract of position is by general possibility restricted to the side of existence, and the contract of predication is by special possibility. And it does not follow from the fact that what is qualified by the description [subject term] by general possibility restricted to the side of existence is qualified by the attribute of the predicate by special possibility, that what is qualified by the attribute of the predicate by general possibility restricted to the side of existence is qualified by the description by special possibility, as is apparent to the considerer. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) And that is the permissibility that the qualification by 'ب' remains in the realm of possibility and never goes out to actuality. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) His statement: 'Since the author chose the view of the Sheikh...' But according to what some investigators among the commentators of the text and some eminent scholars in the Sharh al-Matali' have mentioned—and it is his (the Sheikh's) statement in al-Shifa' and al-Isharat—that what is meant by 'actual' is according to mental presupposition (al-fardh al-'aqli), whether it corresponds to reality or not, then the reflection of them is evident according to his view as well. Because the persistence of the predicate in the realm of possibility does not contradict actuality according to mental presupposition. For it does not contradict our statement 'every ج is ب by possibility' that every thing that can possibly be qualified by 'ج' and the intellect presupposes it as 'ج' actually, whether corresponding to reality or not, is 'ب' by possibility. And it is apparent that what is qualified by 'ب' by possibility is qualified by 'ب' actually according to mental presupposition, for mental actuality does not contradict possibility. Thus, it is true that 'some ب is actually (by mental presupposition) مثل which is 'ج' by possibility', and that is the desired result. There remains something: that the actuality considered in the contract of position according to the view of the Sheikh, if what is meant by it is actuality according to the real state (nafs al-amr) as is well-known among the majority, then this explanation would be specific to cases where the description [subject term] is realized [in reality] and does not apply to cases where no individual exists in reality, such as mental propositions. And if what is meant is actuality by mental presupposition as some have mentioned, then it is objected against: the actuality considered in the contract of predication as in the general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma), either what is meant by it is actuality according to reality or actuality according to mental presupposition. The first is not possible, otherwise their judgment that the general absolute... [text breaks]
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the reflection (aks) of possible propositions, specifically the general possible (mumkina 'amma) and special possible (mumkina khassa). The author first notes that the reflection of the general possible is clear because both contracts are based on general possibility. For the special possible, there is a subtlety: the subject's description is possible generally, but the predicate's description is only specially possible, so the converse does not automatically follow. The author then addresses the well-known difficulty that under Ibn Sina's view (actualism), the two possible propositions do not reflect. However, if we adopt an interpretation of Ibn Sina's 'actuality' as mental presupposition (not necessarily real), then reflection becomes possible. The key argument is that mental actuality does not conflict with possibility; what is possible can be mentally posited as actual. The author ends with a residual issue: whether the actuality in the contract of position is real or mental, and notes that if it is real, the explanation only works for propositions with actual individuals; if mental, then the contract of predication in propositions like the general absolute must also be interpreted consistently. The text breaks off, likely continuing on the next page.

Resolving Non-Reflection via Mental Presupposition
Author resolves non-reflection via mental presupposition of actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how interpreting 'actuality' as mental presupposition (al-fi'l al-fardi) rescues the reflection of possible propositions on Ibn Sina's view.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's possible propositions reflect if actuality is taken as mental rather than real.

The well-known problem is that under Ibn Sina's actualist interpretation, possible propositions (both general and special) do not reflect. However, some commentators suggest that Ibn Sina's 'actuality' refers to mental presupposition, not real existence. This lesson unpacks that solution and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard problem
Under the standard interpretation of Ibn Sina, 'actuality' means real existence (nafs al-amr). In that case, the possible proposition 'Every ج is ب by possibility' claims that ب is possible for ج, but the converse would require ج to be possible for ب, which does not follow because ج may not be actual.
This is the well-known non-reflection of possible propositions.
2
Introduce mental presupposition (al-fi'l al-fardi)
Some commentators interpret 'actuality' in Ibn Sina's texts as mental presupposition: the intellect posits the subject as actual, whether or not it exists in reality.
This interpretation is based on passages from al-Shifa' and al-Isharat.
3
Apply mental presupposition to conversion
If the subject is mentally actual, then the predicate's possibility is not contradicted. In the converse, the predicate (now subject) can also be mentally actual, making the converse true: 'Some ب is ج by possibility'.
Mental actuality does not negate possibility; it merely posits a conceptual instance.
