Refutation of the Conversion of Necessary Absolute Negatives
Author refutes arguments that necessary absolute negatives convert like themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and refute the claim that a necessary absolute negative proposition converts like itself, using the distinction between incompatibility of essence and attribute.

Core Idea
Incompatibility is not always symmetric: essence-to-attribute differs from attribute-to-essence.

In this lesson, we delve into a critical debate in Islamic logic: do necessary absolute negatives (e.g., 'No J is B necessarily') convert into themselves? Some logicians argue yes, giving three arguments. The author of our text systematically refutes each, revealing subtle conditions about syllogistic productivity and the nature of incompatibility (munafāh). Understanding this refutation sharpens your grasp of immediate inference and modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim and the Three Arguments
Find the three arguments presented by those who affirm conversion: reductio using possible premise, conversion of possible, and symmetry of incompatibility.
2
Analyze the First Refutation - Condition of the Minor Premise
Understand why the first figure cannot have a possible minor premise; it requires actuality. The author states that a possible minor is barren.
3
Analyze the Second Refutation - Conversion of General Possible
Note that general possible propositions do not convert. The second argument depends on an invalid conversion.
4
Analyze the Third Refutation - Asymmetry of Incompatibility
Grasp the distinction between incompatibility of essence (dhat) and attribute (sifa). Use the Zayd's mount example: 'Nothing from Zayd's mount is a donkey necessarily' is true, but its converse is false because the incompatibility is between essence and attribute, not symmetric.
5
Contrast al-Farabi and Ibn Sina
See how the underlying ontology of attributes affects the conversion. Under al-Farabi's view, the conversion might hold; under Ibn Sina's, it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال بان الضرورية المطلقة تنعكس كنفسها واستدلوا عليه بالخلف، لانه اذا صدق قولنا : لاشي من ج ب بالضرورة صدق: لاشيء من ب ج بالضرورة والالصدق بعض ب، ج بالامكان العام ونضمه مع الأصل ونقول بعض ب ج بالامكان العام ولا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة ينتج بعض ب ليس ب بالضرورة و هذا محال منشأه نقيض العكس لان الاصل صادق والهيئة منتجة فيكون نقيض العكس باطلاً والعكس حقاً وهـ المطلوب و بالعكس لانه اذا صدق قولنا: لاشيء من ج ب بالضرورة صدق لاشى من ب ج بالضرورة والالصدق بعض ب ، ج بالامكان العام وينعكس الى بعض ج ب بالامكان العام وقد كان حكم الاصل : لاشيء من ج ب بالضرورة. ولا يخفى أن الأول يتوقف على انتاج الصغرى الممكنة فى الشكل الأول وستعرف انها عقيمة لاشتراط الفعلية فيها و ان الثانى يتوقف على انعكاس الممكنة العامة وقد عرفت انها لا تنعكس اصلاً. و استدلوا ايضاً بانا اذا قلنا لاشيء من ج ب بالضرورة كان معناه ان الجيم مناف للباء و المنافاة انما يتحقق من الجانبين فيكون الباء ايضاً منافياً للجيم فيصدق لاشيء من ب، ج بالضرورة و هو المطلوب. وفيه ان معنى الاصل المنافاة بين ذات الجيم ووصف الباء ومعنى العكس المنافاة بين وصف الجيم فاين هذا من ذاك ؟ و ان شئت فاعتبر المثال الذي ذكره المحشى في الحاشية السابقة فانه يصدق لاشيء من مركوب زيد بحمار بالضرورة ولا يصدق: لاشيء من الحمار يمر كوب زيد بالضرورة لصدق نقيضه وهو: بعض الحمار مركوب زيد بالامكان وما هذا الا لان المنافاة في الأصل بين ذات مركوب زيد والحمار و في العكس بين ذات الحمار و وصف مرکوب زید و ظاهرانه لا يلزم من الاول ، الثانى نعم لو قيل: بان اتصاف ذات الموضوع بالوصف العنوانى بالامكان كما هو مذهب الفارابي لا مكن القول بانعكاس السالبة الضرورية كنفسها لصحة انتاج الصغرى الممكنة في الشكل الاول و الجواز انعكاس الممكنة العامة ممكنة عامة على مذهبه وكذا يتم عليه الاستدلال الثالث فان المنافاة وان كانت في الاصل بين ذات ج ووصف ب و فى العكس بالعكس الا ان الاول يستلزم الثاني بناء على ما ذهب اليه الفارابي فانه اذا فرض امتناع الاجتماع بين ذاتج و وصف ب، يلزم ان يكون ذات ب منافياً لذات ج لانه لو كان عينه لزم ان يكون ب صادقاً على ذات ج كما انه يصدق على ذات ب وقد فرض أنه يمتنع الاجتماع بينهما واذا ثبت ان ذات ب مغاير لذات ج، امتنع اتصافه بـ «ج» و الايلزم ان يكون ذات ب عين ذات ج وقد عرفت بطلانه . و اما على ما ذهب اليه الشيخ فلايتم هذا ايضاً اذلا امتناع فى اتصاف ما ليس بذات ج، بج لان معنى الاصل المنافاة بين ذات ج بالفعل و وصف ب و انما يلزم منه ان ذات ب لا يكون ذات ج بالفعل و انه مطلقا حتى بالامكان فتأمل فان هذا المقام يستصعبه اقوام، بالفعل لا انه يمتنع اتصافه يج لانه زحلقة الاقدام (ميراز محمد علی)
And among them are those who said that the necessary absolute [proposition] converts like itself, and they argued by reductio: because if our statement 'No J is B necessarily' is true, then 'No B is J necessarily' is true, and the truth of 'Some B is J by general possibility' is taken, and we combine it with the original and say: Some B is J by general possibility, and nothing from J is B necessarily, which yields that some B is not B necessarily, and this is absurd, whose source is the contradictory of the converse, because the original is true and the syllogism is productive, so the contradictory of the converse is false and the converse is true, and that is the desired result. And conversely: because if our statement 'No J is B necessarily' is true, then 'No B is J necessarily' is true, and the truth of 'Some B is J by general possibility' converts to 'Some J is B by general possibility' while the original ruled 'No J is B necessarily'. And it is not hidden that the first depends on the productivity of the possible minor premise in the first figure, and you will know that it is barren due to the condition of actuality in it, and the second depends on the conversion of the general possible, and you have known that it does not convert at all. And they also argued that when we say 'No J is B necessarily', its meaning is that J is incompatible with B, and incompatibility is only realized from both sides, so B is also incompatible with J, thus 'No B is J necessarily' is true, and that is the desired result. But it is objected that the meaning of the original is incompatibility between the essence of J and the attribute of B, and the meaning of the converse is incompatibility between the attribute of J and the essence of B, so where is this from that? And if you wish, consider the example mentioned by the commentator in the previous gloss: for it is true that 'Nothing from Zayd's mount is a donkey necessarily', and it is not true that 'Nothing from the donkey is Zayd's mount necessarily' due to the truth of its contradictory, which is 'Some donkey is Zayd's mount by possibility', and this is only because the incompatibility in the original is between the essence of Zayd's mount and the donkey, and in the converse it is between the essence of the donkey and the attribute of Zayd's mount, and it is apparent that the second does not follow from the first. Yes, if it were said that the essence of the subject is described by the attributive description by possibility, as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, then it would be possible to say that the negative necessary converts like itself, due to the validity of the production of the possible minor premise in the first figure, and the permissibility of the conversion of the general possible as a general possible according to his doctrine, and likewise the third argument would be valid upon him: for the incompatibility, even if in the original it is between the essence of J and the attribute of B, and in the converse the opposite, yet the first entails the second based on what al-Farabi held. For if the impossibility of conjunction between the essence of J and the attribute of B is assumed, it follows that the essence of B is incompatible with the essence of J, because if it were identical, it would necessitate that B is true of the essence of J just as it is true of the essence of B, and it has been assumed that conjunction between them is impossible. And when it is established that the essence of B is other than the essence of J, it becomes impossible for it to be described by 'J', otherwise it would necessitate that the essence of B is the essence of J, and you have known its falsehood. But according to what the Master (al-Shaykh) held, this also does not hold, because there is no impossibility in something that is not the essence of J being described by J, since the meaning of the original is incompatibility between the actual essence of J and the attribute of B, and it only follows from that that the essence of B is not the actual essence of J, and that it is absolutely [not the actual essence] even by possibility, so reflect, for this station is difficult for people, indeed it is not that its being described by J is impossible, but rather that its being described by J is a slippery slope. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the claim that the necessary absolute negative proposition ('No J is B necessarily') converts into itself ('No B is J necessarily'). The author presents arguments by some logicians for this conversion, then refutes them. The first argument uses reductio: assume the original is true, then take a possible affirmative converse ('Some B is J by general possibility'), combine it with the original in first figure to deduce an absurdity, thereby proving the converse. The author counters that this depends on the possible minor premise being productive in the first figure, which is denied because the first figure requires actuality in the minor premise. The second argument relies on the conversion of the general possible, which is not allowed. The third argument claims that incompatibility is symmetrical. The author refutes this by distinguishing between incompatibility of essence with attribute (in the original) and of attribute with essence (in the converse), giving the example of Zayd's mount and donkey: nothing that is Zayd's mount is a donkey necessarily, but some donkeys can be Zayd's mount possibly, because the incompatibility is between the essence of 'Zayd's mount' and the attribute 'donkey', while the converse would require incompatibility between the essence of donkey and the attribute 'Zayd's mount', which does not hold. The author then notes that if one follows al-Farabi's view that the subject's essence is described by the attributive description only by possibility, then the conversion might be valid, but not according to the doctrine of al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina). This shows the importance of determining the ontological status of attributes in logical conversions.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
المنافاة
الممكنة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the third argument for conversion (based on symmetry of incompatibility) fail?
A) Because incompatibility is never symmetric.
B) Because the original has incompatibility between essence of J and attribute of B, while the converse requires incompatibility between attribute of J and essence of B, which does not follow.
C) Because the syllogism requires the minor premise to be actual, not possible.
D) Because the general possible does not convert.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of necessary absolute negatives fails because incompatibility between essence and attribute is not symmetric, and the required syllogistic conditions are not met.

The Three Interpretations of the Conditional General and Their Conversion
Analysis of conversion conditions for the conditional general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three interpretations of the conditional general proposition (mādām, bi-sharṭ, li-ajl) and determine when conversion is valid.

Core Idea
The validity of converting a conditional general depends on whether the description is a cause or a mere condition.

This passage refines our understanding of the conversion (عکس) of a special type of categorical proposition: the conditional general. The author shows that the conversion rule is not uniform; it hinges on how the necessity is understood relative to the subject's description. We will dissect the three interpretations and see why only one yields a valid conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The conditional general (المشروطة العامة) is a categorical proposition that asserts necessity of the relation as long as a certain description of the subject endures. For example, 'Every A is B as long as A is described by C.' Identify the description (wasf).
2
Distinguish the three interpretations
The phrase 'as long as' can mean: (1) temporal co-existence (mādām al-wasf), (2) a necessary condition (bi-sharṭ al-wasf), or (3) a cause (li-ajl al-wasf). Notice how each changes the logical relationship between the attributes.
The author uses Zayd's mount as an example: 'Zayd's mount is not a donkey as long as it is Zayd's mount.'
3
Test conversion under mādām and bi-sharṭ
Under the temporal or conditional interpretations, the converse 'Nothing that is a donkey is Zayd's mount as long as it is a donkey' does not follow. The incompatibility is only between the predicate attribute and the subject essence, not between the two attributes universally.
In the example, some donkeys could be Zayd's mount, making the converse false.
4
Test conversion under li-ajl
When the description is the cause (li-ajl al-wasf), the necessity stems from the description itself. This creates a direct incompatibility between the two descriptions (subject's and predicate's), making the converse valid.
The author says: 'just as it is valid to judge incompatibility of predicate's attribute with subject's essence because of subject's description, similarly it is valid to judge incompatibility of subject's attribute with predicate's essence because of predicate's description.'
5
Summarize the condition for valid conversion
Conversion of a conditional general like itself (i.e., preserving the 'as long as' clause) is only valid if the description is the cause (li-ajl). In other cases, conversion fails because the required absolute incompatibility between attributes is missing.
This nuanced rule prevents invalid inferences in logical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخفى ان عدم انعكاس المشروطة العامة كنفسها انما يصح اذا فسرت المشروطة العامة بما حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة مادام الوصف فانه لا يلزم من منافاة وصف المحمول لذات الموضوع في جميع اوقات وصف الموضوع المنافاة بين وصفى الموضوع والمحمول مطلقا حتى يلزم من صدق احدهما على شيء انتفاء الاخر. نعم يلزم المنافاة بينهما فى ذات الموضوع خاصة و اين هذا من مفهوم العكس؟ فان مفهومه المنافاة بين ذات المحمول ووصف الموضوع فى جميع اوقات وصف المحمول ولا يستلزم احدهما الآخر لجواز تغاير ذات المحمول لذات الموضوع كما اذا فرض ان مركوب زيد بالفعل منحصر في الفرس فانه يصدق ح : الضرورة لا شيء من الحمار بالضرورة لاشيء من مركوب زيد بحمار مادام مركوب زيد ولا يصدق بالفروق كذا اذا فسرت ما بمركوب زيد مادام حماراً لصدق نقيضه وهو: بعض الحمار مركوب زيد بالامكان و هكذا اذا فسرت بما حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة بشرط الوصف لانه لا يلزم من منافاة مجموع ذات الموضوع و وصفه لوصف المحمول المنافاة بين مجموع ذات المحمول ووصفه وبين وصف الموضوع كما في المثال المذكور و اما اذا فسرت بما حكم فيها بضرورة النسبة لاجل الوصف فالظاهر انه يصحح انعكاسها كنفسها فان كون الوصف منشأ للضرورة كما هو مفهومها ح يدل على تحقق المنافاة بين الوصفين فح كما يصح الحكم بمنافاة وصف المحمول لذات الموضوع لاجل وصف الموضوع كذلك يصح الحكم بمنافاة وصف الموضوع الذات المحمول لاجل وصف المحمول وهو مفهوم العكس (میرزا محمد علی)
It is not hidden that the non-conversion of the conditional general like itself is only valid when the conditional general is interpreted as that in which it is judged that the relation is necessary as long as the description (mādām al-wasf) exists. For from the incompatibility of the predicate's attribute with the subject's essence in all times of the subject's description, the incompatibility between the two attributes (subject and predicate) absolutely does not follow, such that from the truth of one about something the negation of the other would follow. Yes, the incompatibility between them in the subject's essence specifically follows, and where is this from the concept of conversion? For its concept is the incompatibility between the predicate's essence and the subject's attribute in all times of the predicate's description, and neither entails the other due to the possibility of the predicate's essence being different from the subject's essence, as when it is assumed that Zayd's mount is actually restricted to the horse, then it is true by necessity that nothing from the donkey is [Zayd's mount?], nothing from Zayd's mount is a donkey as long as it is Zayd's mount, and it is not true that nothing from Zayd's mount is a donkey as long as it is a donkey, due to the truth of its contradictory, which is: some donkey is Zayd's mount by possibility. And likewise when it is interpreted as that in which the necessity of the relation is judged with the condition of the description (bi-sharṭ al-wasf), because from the incompatibility of the totality of the subject's essence and its description with the predicate's description, the incompatibility between the totality of the predicate's essence and its description and the subject's description does not follow, as in the mentioned example. But when it is interpreted as that in which the necessity of the relation is judged because of the description (li-ajl al-wasf), then apparently its conversion like itself is valid. For the fact that the description is the source of the necessity, as is its concept, indicates the realization of incompatibility between the two descriptions. So just as it is valid to judge the incompatibility of the predicate's attribute with the subject's essence because of the subject's description, similarly it is valid to judge the incompatibility of the subject's attribute with the predicate's essence because of the predicate's description, and that is the concept of conversion. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage examines the conversion of the 'conditional general' (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma), a proposition where the relation is necessary as long as the subject's description holds. The author states that the failure of conversion (i.e., it does not convert like itself) applies only when the proposition is interpreted as 'necessary as long as the description exists' (mādām al-wasf). In that case, the incompatibility between the predicate's attribute and the subject's essence does not imply incompatibility between the two attributes (subject and predicate) in general, which would be needed for conversion. For example, if Zayd's mount is only a horse, then 'Nothing from Zayd's mount is a donkey as long as it is Zayd's mount' is true, but 'Nothing from Zayd's mount is a donkey as long as it is a donkey' is false because some donkeys could be Zayd's mount. The author then distinguishes two other interpretations: (1) 'with the condition of the description' (bi-sharṭ al-wasf), where the necessity holds only while the description is present, and the same issue arises; (2) 'because of the description' (li-ajl al-wasf), where the description is the cause of the necessity, and here the conversion is valid because the incompatibility is between the two descriptions directly. This nuanced analysis shows how subtle differences in interpreting quantified propositions affect logical conversions.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
مادام الوصف
بشرط الوصف
لأجل الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which interpretation of the conditional general does conversion like itself become valid?
A) When interpreted as 'necessary as long as the description exists'.
B) When interpreted as 'necessary with the condition of the description'.
C) When interpreted as 'necessary because of the description'.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a conditional general proposition is only valid when the description is the cause of the necessity, not a mere temporal condition.

?

Converting Durative Negatives to Particular Affirmatives
Commentary on conversion of negative specifics using 'urfiyya vs. dawam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a durative negative proposition (a universal negative) into a particular affirmative converse, following the rule that the durative negative is equivalent to a universal affirmative which converts only to a particular.

Core Idea
A 'durative negative' is a universal affirmative in disguise, converting only to a particular.

In this passage, the commentator refines the author's discussion of converting two negative specific propositions. The key is that a durative negative (always not) is equivalent to a universal affirmative (every is not), and such a universal affirmative converts only to a particular affirmative. Understanding this nuance ensures correct conversion in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the negative proposition is 'urfiyya (customary, holds normally) or dā'ima (durative, always). This passage focuses on the durative negative.
The durative negative asserts 'always not', e.g., 'No intoxicant is ever permissible'.
2
Convert durative negative to universal affirmative
Recognize that a durative negative (e.g., 'No S is P') is logically equivalent to a universal affirmative with a negated predicate (e.g., 'Every S is not-P'). This is an obversion step.
This equivalence is standard: 'No S is P' = 'Every S is not-P'.
3
Apply conversion to the universal affirmative
A universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative ('Some not-P is S'). This is the standard rule: 'Every S is not-P' converts to 'Some not-P is S'.
Universal affirmatives do not convert to universal affirmatives; they convert to particulars.
4
State the final converse
Thus, the converse of a durative negative is a particular affirmative. For example, from 'No khamr is halal' (durative), we obtain 'Some haram is khamr' (particular affirmative).
The converse is a particular affirmative proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وقال المصنف: «السرفى ذلك» اى فى قولنا عرفية لادائمة في البعض في عكس الخاصتين السالبتين « ان لادوام السالبة موجبة كلية وهى لا تنعكس الاجزئية و عليه فيكون عكس
His saying: And the author said: 'The secret in that' meaning in our saying 'customary, not durative in some' in the conversion of the two negative specific [propositions] 'that the durative of the negative is a universal affirmative, and it does not convert except to a particular, and accordingly the converse would be...'
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This brief passage is a comment on the author's (al-musannif) statement regarding the conversion of two negative specific propositions. The author says 'the secret in that' refers to the statement that the proposition is 'urfiyya (customary) not durative (dā'ima) in some aspect, in the conversion of the two negative specifics. He then clarifies that the durative negative (which asserts always not) is equivalent to a universal affirmative (by obversion), and such a universal affirmative does not convert except to a particular. Therefore, the converse would be particular. This is a standard point about conversion: A universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, so an equivalent durative negative converts accordingly.

Key Terms

الدوام (al-dawām)
سالبة كلية (universal negative)
موجبة كلية (universal affirmative)
جزئية موجبة (particular affirmative)

Key Takeaway
A durative negative (always not) is logically equivalent to a universal affirmative, and its converse is a particular affirmative.

The Error in the Author's Secret for Converting Particular Negatives
Discusses the conversion of particular negative propositions and evaluates the author's explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the conversion of a composite proposition does not reduce to the conversion of its parts, using the commentator's objection.

Core Idea
Composite propositions convert as wholes, not as sums of parts.

In this passage, the author presents a 'secret' behind the conversion of particular negative propositions, claiming that since the non-permanence part is a universal affirmative that doesn't convert to a particular, the overall conversion is a particular conditional. The commentator objects, arguing that this reasoning fails because the conversion of the composite is not dependent on the conversion of its parts. Let's unpack this debate and learn a crucial principle about immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the author's statement about the 'secret' (السر) in converting particular negatives. He argues that the non-permanence of the negative is a universal affirmative, which doesn't convert to a particular, so the overall conversion becomes a particular conditional.
The author's secret is based on decomposing the composite into parts and converting each part.
2
Extract the author's logical reasoning
The author decomposes the composite proposition into quantifier and modality, converts each separately, and combines them. This assumes that the conversion of the whole is the combination of the conversions of its parts.
3
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator says: 'ليس انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء' – the conversion of the composite to the composite is not dependent on the conversion of parts to parts.
This is the core principle.
4
Analyze the counterexample
The commentator provides an example: affirmative particular negatives (الخاصتين الموجبتين) convert to a temporal conditional, even though the non-permanence part (absolute universal negative) has no conversion according to the author's own foundation. This shows that the author's part-wise approach fails.
The counterexample directly refutes the author's claim.
5
State the general principle
The conversion of a composite proposition must be considered as a whole, not by simply combining the conversions of its parts. The commentator advocates analyzing the composite into its simple components, converting each simple proposition individually, and then recombining them.
This principle is essential for correct immediate inference in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وقال المصنف: «السرفى ذلك» اى فى قولنا عرفية لادائمة في البعض في عكس الخاصتين السالبتين « ان لادوام السالبة موجبة كلية وهى لا تنعكس الاجزئية و عليه فيكون عكس الخاصتين السالبتين عرفية خاصة ولا دوامها بعضى يعنى ان قولنا: لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع بالضرورة او بالدوام مادام كاتباً لا دائماً، عكس اصله : لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً ما دام ساكن الاصابع، ولا دوام الاصل يشير الى قولنا كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ولا دوام العكس يشير الى قولنا بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب بالفعل. و هذه الموجبة الجزئية المأخوذة من لادوام العكس، عكس للموجبة الكلية المأخوذة في لادوام الاصل. و اورد المحشى على الماتن بقوله: و فيه تأمل اذ ليس انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع » كانعكاس الخاصتين بما هما خاصتان من غير نظر الى لا دوامها منفرداً الى عرفية خاصة بماهي عرفية خاصة من غير نظر الى لا دوامها منفرداً منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء » كما فعلنا نحن بالنسبة الى انعكاس المركبات حيث حللنا قيودها و ارجعناها الى مراجعها من القضايا البسيطة و عكسنا قضية الاصل البسيطة وقضية القيد البسيطة «كما يشهد بذلك إلى بان انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع ليس منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء ملاحظة انعكاس الموجهات الموجبة على مامر فان الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان ( الى الحينية اللادائمة مع ان الجزء الثانى منهما أي من الخاصتين الموجبتين « وهو: » اللادوام الذى مرجعه ( المطلقة العامة السالبة لاعكس لها على مبنى المصنف فاذا كان انعكاس المجموع الى المجموع لايناط بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء فما ذكره المصنف من السر غير صحيح و ايراد المحشي وارد على الماتن انصافاً (التقريب ص ٧٢)
His saying: 'And the author said: The secret in that' – i.e., in our statement 'a particular non-permanent conditional' in the conversion of two particular negatives – 'is that the non-permanence of the negative is a universal affirmative, and it does not convert to a particular, and accordingly the conversion of the two particular negatives is a particular conditional; and its non-permanence is partial' – meaning that our statement: 'Nothing of the writer is still-fingered necessarily or always as long as he is a writer, not always' – its conversion is its original: 'Nothing of the still-fingered is a writer always as long as it is still-fingered', and the non-permanence of the original refers to our statement 'Every writer is actually still-fingered', and the non-permanence of the conversion refers to our statement 'Some still-fingered is actually a writer'. And this particular affirmative taken from the non-permanence of the conversion is the conversion of the universal affirmative taken in the non-permanence of the original. And the commentator (al-muḥashshī) objected to the author (al-mātin) by saying: 'There is consideration in this, for the conversion of the composite to the composite is not like the conversion of two particular negatives insofar as they are two particular negatives without regard to their non-permanence individually into a particular conditional insofar as it is a particular conditional without regard to its non-permanence individually, dependent on the conversion of parts to parts.' As we did with respect to the conversion of composites, where we analyzed their conditions and referred them to their references of simple propositions, and we converted the simple original proposition and the simple condition proposition. This is witnessed by the fact that the conversion of the composite to the composite is not dependent on the conversion of parts to parts – observe the conversion of affirmative modals as has passed. For the two affirmative particular negatives convert to a temporal non-permanent (conditional), while the second part of them, i.e., the non-permanence whose reference is the absolute universal negative – has no conversion according to the author's foundation. So if the conversion of the composite to the composite is not dependent on the conversion of parts to parts, then what the author mentioned as the secret is incorrect, and the objection of the commentator is validly directed at the author. (al-Taqrib, p. 72)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents the author's (المصنف) explanation of the 'secret' behind the conversion of particular negative propositions (الخاصتين السالبتين) into a particular conditional. The author states that the non-permanence (لادوام) of a negative gives a universal affirmative, and since a universal affirmative does not convert to a particular, the conversion of the particular negative yields a particular conditional. The commentator (المحشى) objects, arguing that the conversion of a composite proposition to a composite does not depend on separately converting its parts (the quantifier and the condition). He provides a counterexample: affirmative particular negatives convert to a temporal conditional, even though one part (the non-permanence) is a universal negative that has no conversion. Hence the author's reasoning is flawed. This illustrates the principle that the whole conversion behavior is not simply reducible to the conversion of components.

Key Terms
الخاصتين السالبتين
لادوام
المجموع إلى المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the author did not consider the universal negative part

C) Because particular negatives never convert
D) Because the example given was false

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a composite proposition is not reducible to the conversion of its parts; the whole has its own conversion rules.

?

5 min
Non-conversion of Particular Conditionals
Notes that when both parts are particular, the second part is a particular negative which does not convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a conditional with two particular parts does not convert by applying the rule of non-conversion of particular negatives.

Core Idea
Particular negatives do not convert; therefore a conditional with two particular parts cannot convert.

In this brief commentary note, the author clarifies a specific case of conversion in conditional propositions. When both parts of a conditional are particular (juz'iyya), the second part becomes a particular negative. Since particular negatives never convert, the entire conditional is not convertible. This rule is essential for correctly handling conditional propositions in logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a conditional
Recognize the antecedent and consequent of the conditional proposition.
2
Check if both are particular
Determine if each part is a particular proposition (e.g., 'Some A is B' or 'Some A is not B').
3
Apply the conversion rule
If the second part is a particular negative, recall that particular negatives do not convert. Hence the conditional as a whole does not convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ايضاً اذا كانتا جزئيتين يكون الجزء الثانى سالبة جزئية مع انها لا تنعكس كما تقدم و يحتمل ان يكون هذا وجهاً لقوله: «قندير». (محمد علی)
And also, if they are two particulars, then the second part is a particular negative, and indeed it does not convert, as has preceded. And it is possible that this is a reason for his saying: 'Qandīr'. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This brief note adds another point: when both parts of a conditional proposition are particular (وجودية), the second part becomes a particular negative (سالبة جزئية). Since particular negatives do not convert (as established earlier), the whole conditional cannot convert. The commentator suggests that this may be the basis for a statement attributed to 'Qandīr' (possibly a logician's name).

Key Terms
جزئية
قندير

Checkpoint
What is the reason given for the non-conversion when both parts are particular?
A) The first part converts but the second does not
B) The second part is a universal negative
C) The second part is a particular negative which does not convert
D) Both parts are universal

Key Takeaway
A conditional with two particular parts cannot convert because the second part is a particular negative, which never converts.

8 min
Critique of Composite Modal Proposition Conversion
Commentator objects to author's composite conversion method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the method of converting composite modal propositions by analyzing the interaction of their parts and the validity of their independent converses.

Core Idea
Composite conversion fails when the parts have no independent converse.

In classical Islamic logic, modal propositions carry temporal or conditional restrictions. Converting such composites requires more than converting each part separately; the parts may interact or lack a valid converse. The commentator here challenges the author's method, showing that the conversion of the whole is not simply the sum of its parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Modal Propositions
The passage begins with two negative specific propositions (khāṣṣa sāliba) that are 'particularized conventional' with partial non-permanence. Recognize that these are composite modal propositions: 'Nothing from the writer is with still fingers necessarily or perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually'.
2
Extract Original and Converse
From the text, extract the original proposition and its alleged converse: original: 'Nothing from the writer is with still fingers necessarily/perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually'; converse: 'Nothing from the still-fingered is a writer perpetually as long as he is still-fingered.' Write them down.
3
Analyze the Non-Permanence Components
The 'lā dawām' (non-permanence) of the original refers to the affirmative 'Every writer is with still fingers actually'; the non-permanence of the converse refers to 'Some still-fingered are writers actually.' Note that this latter is a particular affirmative, which is the converse of the universal affirmative in the original's non-permanence.
4
Understand the Commentator's Objection
The commentator argues that converting the composite whole to the whole is not like converting the specific propositions as such without regard to their non-permanence individually. He says the conversion of composites should be done by analyzing their restrictions into simple propositions and converting each simple part—but here, the second part of positive specific propositions (the non-permanence) refers to the general absolute negative, which has no conversion according to the author's own principles. Therefore, the author's claimed 'secret' is incorrect.
5
Consider the Example of Positive Specifics and the Final Response
The commentator adds that positive specific propositions convert to 'existential non-perpetual' while their second part (non-permanence) has no converse. If both parts are particular, the second part is a particular negative, which also does not convert. Finally, al-Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim responds that the converse is not necessarily true in all materials; proving it in some materials does not refute the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصتين السالبتين عرفية خاصة ولا دوامها بعضى يعنى ان قولنا: لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع بالضرورة او بالدوام مادام كاتباً لا دائماً، عكس اصله : لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً ما دام ساكن الاصابع، ولا دوام الاصل يشير الى قولنا كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ولا دوام العكس يشير الى قولنا بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب بالفعل. و هذه الموجبة الجزئية المأخوذة من لادوام العكس، عكس للموجبة الكلية المأخوذة في لادوام الاصل. و اورد المحشى على الماتن بقوله: و فيه تأمل اذ ليس انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع » كانعكاس الخاصتين بما هما خاصتان من غير نظر الى لا دوامها منفرداً الى عرفية خاصة بماهي عرفية خاصة من غير نظر الى لا دوامها منفرداً منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء » كما فعلنا نحن بالنسبة الى انعكاس المركبات حيث حللنا قيودها و ارجعناها الى مراجعها من القضايا البسيطة و عكسنا قضية الاصل البسيطة وقضية القيد البسيطة «كما يشهد بذلك إلى بان انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع ليس منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء ملاحظة انعكاس الموجهات الموجبة على مامر فان الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان ( الى الحينية اللادائمة مع ان الجزء الثانى منهما أي من الخاصتين الموجبتين « وهو: » اللادوام الذى مرجعه ( المطلقة العامة السالبة لاعكس لها على مبنى المصنف فاذا كان انعكاس المجموع الى المجموع لايناط بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء فما ذكره المصنف من السر غير صحيح و ايراد المحشي وارد على الماتن انصافاً (التقريب ص ٧٢) (۳۲) و ايضاً اذا كانتا جزئيتين يكون الجزء الثانى سالبة جزئية مع انها لا تنعكس كما تقدم و يحتمل ان يكون هذا وجهاً لقوله: «قندير». (محمد علی) (قال الشيخ عبد الرحيم) اى ليس لها عکس لازم الصدق فى جميع المواد فاثبات العكس في بعض المواد لا يدفع الايراد.
The two negative specific [propositions] are particularized conventional and their non-permanence is partial, meaning that our statement: 'Nothing from the writer is with still fingers necessarily or perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' is the converse of its original: 'Nothing from the still-fingered is a writer perpetually as long as he is still-fingered.' And the non-permanence of the original refers to our statement: 'Every writer is with still fingers actually,' and the non-permanence of the converse refers to our statement: 'Some still-fingered are writers actually.' And this particular affirmative taken from the non-permanence of the converse is the converse of the universal affirmative taken in the non-permanence of the original. The commentator objected to the author saying: 'There is reflection in it, for the conversion of the composite whole to the whole is not like the conversion of the two specific propositions as such without regard to their non-permanence individually to a particularized conventional as such without regard to its non-permanence individually, dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as we did concerning the conversion of composites when we analyzed their restrictions and referred them to their referents from simple propositions and we converted the simple original proposition and the simple restriction proposition. This is witnessed by the fact that the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, observing the conversion of positive modal propositions as previously, for the two positive specific propositions convert to the existential non-perpetual while the second part of them, i.e., of the two positive specific propositions, which is the non-permanence whose referent is the general absolute negative, has no conversion according to the author's construction. So if the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, then what the author mentioned as the secret is incorrect, and the objection of the commentator is valid against the author, fairly. (al-Taqrib p. 72) (32) And also if they are two particular, the second part is a particular negative while it does not convert as previously, and it is possible that this is a face for his saying: 'Qandir'. (Muhammad Ali) (al-Shaykh Abd al-Rahim said): i.e., it does not have a converse necessarily true in all materials, so proving the converse in some materials does not repel the objection.'
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the conversion of composite modal propositions. It begins by explaining the conversion of two negative specific propositions using an example: 'Nothing from the writer is with still fingers necessarily or perpetually as long as he is a writer, not perpetually.' The original and its converse are given, along with the analysis of their non-permanence components. The commentator then objects to the author's method: the conversion of the composite whole to the whole is not simply the conversion of its parts independently. He argues that positive specific propositions convert to 'existential non-perpetual' while their second part (the non-permanence) refers to the general absolute negative, which has no conversion according to the author's own principles. Therefore, the author's claimed 'secret' (sirr) is flawed. An additional point is made about particular propositions: if both parts are particular, the second part becomes a particular negative, which does not convert. Finally, al-Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim responds that the converse is not necessarily true in all materials; thus, proving the converse in some materials does not refute the objection. The discussion underscores the complexity of converting composite propositions and the need to consider the interaction of their parts.

Key Terms
خاصة سالبة
عرفية خاصة
لا دوام
انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع
قيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the author's method for converting composite modal propositions fail in the case of positive specific propositions?
A) Because the original proposition is negative and cannot be converted.
B) Because the second part of the positive specific (the non-permanence) refers to a general absolute negative, which has no valid converse.
C) Because the non-permanence of the original and converse do not match.
D) Because the author forgot to include a temporal restriction.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of composite modal propositions requires that each part individually has a valid converse; if a part lacks a converse, the composite conversion fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Take the composite modal proposition: 'Nothing from the fasting person is eating food necessarily as long as he is fasting.' Following the commentator's method, decompose it into simple propositions (the universal negative and the temporal condition). Attempt to convert each part independently. Does the conversion succeed overall? Explain why or why not.
?
Task 2: In the passage, the commentator says positive specific propositions convert to 'existential non-perpetual' while their non-permanence (the second part) has no converse. Use the example from the passage: 'Every writer is with still fingers actually' (a universal affirmative with actual non-permanence). Check if this proposition itself has a valid converse. What does the author's rule about the general absolute negative predict?
